COL. JAS. F. SMITH
MADE A BRIGADIER
The Fighting Leader of the Fight
ing First California Regiment
Gets His Just Dues at Last.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. WELLINGTON HOTEL. WASHINGTON,
D. C, April 24. â President McKinley to-day officially recognized
and rewarded the gallant services of Colonel James F. Smith, com
manding the First California Regiment, by making him brigadier gen
eral of volunteers. The Secretary of War said to the Call correspondent
to-night that Colonel Smith's services had been brought to the Presi
dent's attention by the War Department, and that he was regarded high
ly both by President McKinley and himself. The Secretary stated that
Colonel Smith's promotion was made in recognition of his "valiant
service in the Philippines." The War Department realizes that a great
many, if not the majority of volunteers, in the Philippines will desire to
come home, but hopes that general officers will elect to remain there
and that Brigadier General Smith will be of this number.
The Call correspondent to-night tried again to get a definite state
ment from the Secretary of War as to the time the California regiment
may be relieved. The Seci-etary said Otis had not informed him.
"All I can say is that the First California will be the first regiment
mustered out." said he, "and it will be sent home as soon as relieved by
a regular regiment and transportation facilities are provided."

nandina.

miles long, are, it Is feared, not suffi
ciently strong and compact to main
tain possession against the overwhelm
ing odds which may confront them
upon any trying occasion. All these
things are not adrr'Heri at the War De
partment, nor yet are tV â  strenuously
denied.
The signal service corps of the War
Department is pushing the work pre
liminary to laying cables.
The following order was issued to
day: Second lieutenant Walter L.
Clarke will continue on duty with the
Philippine cable expedition, with tem
porary station at New York City, until
that expedition starts for Manila.
FILIPINOS SEIZE
AMERICAN SHIPS
VICTORIA, B. C, April 24.â The Ma
nila Times of March 28, received per
K. M. S. Empress of China, says the
United States gunboat Yorktown has
discovered the long missing trading
steamer San Joaquin hidden in a. creek
near Dugapan. She left Manila on
January 10 for Cagay.-m and Aparri,
and when she was betweeen the ports
of San Fernando and Currimao the
Filipino crew mutined. They murdered
the Spanish captain and the chief en
gineer and took charge of ti-.. ship, the
mates and other officers having es
caped in a launch. They ran the steam
er up the creek and drove her ashore
on a smooth beach. They then looted
her, and after disabling her engines
and demolishing the staterooms, car
ried away all that was valuable in her
cargo.
The long overdue steamers Gloria
and Saturnup also are back at Manila,
The Gloria left on January 10 for Casa
yan and Currimao and the Saturnus
on February 1 for San Fernando and
Aparri. Upon the arrival of each at
Currimao armed insurgents boarded
her and the vessel was ordered to re
main in port. Their cargoes and all
the food they had were taken by the
insurgents, and to cap the climax the
crews deserted, leaving no one to man
the ships. On the insurgents allowing
them to leave port on March 6, the
Gloria left with only the captain, en
gineer and one sailor to man the ship,'
fearing the insurgents would change
their minds and not let them go. The
SAN FRANCISCO, TUESDAY, APRIL 25, 1899.
Saturnus got fifteen men. and on the
trip the chief engineer changed now
and again to the Gloria to help.
The insurgents took all the guns
from th<- ships. At present, the cap
tains report, the natives at Currimao
are arrfled with Mausers, Remingtons
and bolos. They say they will never
Surrender to the Americans.
On March 30 the Yorktown seized
the Spanish steamer Mundara, says
the Manila Times, off the Gulf of Ling
ayen. When first sighted by the York
town the Mundara was entering the
gulf. She turned and ran to sea. The
Yorktown gave chase and fired a six
pounder across her bow and she hove
to. She was brought to Manila. She
is said to have been attempting to fili
buster arms to the Filipinos.
News is given from Leyte Island
that natives of the town of Maltibog
have seized the steamer Victoria, mur
dered the captain and engineer and
captured Mr. Cogan McLeod and the
company's agent, holding them for
ransom. Her Majesty's ship Plover
has gone to the scene.
There still are several American ves
sels missing, among which are the
Adelante, the Don Jose, the Herminia
and the San Pedro.
FILIPINOS HOIST
A BRITISH FLAG
VICTORIA, B. C, April 24.â News
was brought by R. M. S. Empress of
China that H. M. S. Archer, which
sailed on February 26 with sealed or
ders, ha-, returned to Singapore. She
went direct from there to Palawan.
Her orders were received from Eng
land by telegraph. They were to pro
ceed as quickly as possible to Palawan,
where the British flc.g had been hoisted
by the natives. The Archer's mission
was to haul it down.
On arriving off a small settlement on
the Palawan coast the British red en
sign was seen flying, and the ship had
not been at anchor any length of time
before several Filipinos came aboard
and had an interview with Command
er Dare. He informed them he had
been instructed to take the flag down
and that it would be his duty to do
so. Some palaver ensued, in the course
of which it became apparent that the
inhabitants of the town had hoisted
the British flag because they thought
it might afford them protection from
bands of brigands who were ensconsed

MURDERER
MOORE IS
CAPTURED
Taken Single-Handed by
Sheriff Veale of Contra
Costa County.
OFFICER'S BRAVE ACT
Goes Cut Alone to Meet the Fugi
tive, Who Was Tramping
Toward Martinez,
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MARTINEZ, April 24.â Without a
doubt Ed Moore, the murderer of Po
liceman Kilroy of Nevada city, is now
lodged in the tanks in the County Jaii
here. The fellow answers the descrip
tion exactly, and he had with him the
Colts revolver of the dead officer. Be
sides he had a sold hunting ease watch
with the initials "I. M. ' engraved on
it. Sheriff Getchell of Nevada City will
arrive here to-morow and take the
murderer back with him.
The credit for the capture belongs t>>
Sheriff R. R. Veale. who accomplished
the feat unaided. Last night a sup
posed tramp called at the farm house
of Janus Kennerly at Bay Point, about
four miles east of Martinez, and asked
if he might sleep under a tree, as he
had walked a great deal that day and
was very tired. 1 Kennerly asked him
why he did not sleep in the small open
railroad station at that point, and he
answered he was afraid the officers
J might arrest him as a hobo. Kennerly
! then told the stranger to sleep in the
barn.
During this conversaton the tramp
was very nervous, and this aroused
I the suspicion of Kennerly. Later in
! the evening a neighbor named Wilds
] came in, and they talked the matter
i over. Both came to the conclusion that
! the tramp must be wanted somewhere.
The more they talked the more con
vinced they became of this. They de
cided to be prepared for anything that
might happen in the morning, and so
loaded several guns about the place.
This morning Kennerly and Wilds
met again, and, on further discussion,
both agreed thai th stranger was none
other than Moore, the murderer of Offi
cer Kilroy of Xevuda City. To be cer
tain of the matter they hunted up a
copy of The Call containing Moore's
picture, and after looking at it and
comparing it with the features of the
stranger, all their doubts were re
moved. After this talk th?y went nut
to the barn where the fellow was lying
down.
Finally Moore got up. shook the hay
from himself, went to the door of the
barn and took a look all around. He
went back, threw his blankets over his
shoulder, picked up his gun, cocked it,
and then started off at a rapid gait
down the railroad track toward Mar
tinez.
Kennerly immediately hitched up a
team and drove to Black Diamond, a
small town about six miles distant, and
telephoned to Sheriff Veale, informing
him of what had occurred. The Sheriff
was in doubt about the identification at
first, but told Kennerly he would go
out to meet the stranger. Kennerly
informed Sheriff Veale piat as Moore
left Bay Point at about 8 o'clock this
morning he must be almost to Mar
tinez.
It was now getting well along to 10
o'clock and Sheriff Veale started two
of his deputies up the railroad track
along the bay shore to meet the
stranger. At 10:42 o'clock Sheriff Veale
took the Stockton train himself. He
was armed with a Winchester repeat
ing rifle of the latest pattern. Getting
on the train, he met a friend named
Samuels. Both men went into the bag
gage car, one taking a position at
either door on opposite sides. In a
short time the train stopped at a small
flag station a few miles above Mar
MOORE, THE SLAYER OF KILROY.
NEGRO PREACHER THE
VICTIM OF LYNCHERS
GOVERNOR A. D. CANDLER OF GEORGIA.
tinez. Veale looked up the track and
saw a man walking toward the train.
He concluded it was Moore and jumped
off.
Henry Bonzagni. a 14-year-old boy,
who resides here, was on the train, go
ing to visit his sister, and he got off at
this station and started up the track.
'Come back here, Bonzagni!" shouted
Veale.
This the boy did, and the two got into
the station out of sight and through a
knothole watched the advance of the
stranger. In the meantime the Sheriff
put his rifle together. He waited until
Moore was within about forty feet of
him. Then he stepped out, leveled th"
rifle At the murderer and commanded:
"Hands up."
As Moore is hard of hearing he did
not at first understand what was said
to him, but he stopped walking.
"Hands up, or I will shoot you! I
am an officer!" shouted Veale.
At this Moore dropped his bundle and
his shotgun and raised his hands. Veale
told Bonzagni to search Moore. The
boy did so, and in a few moments
pulled out a Smith & Wesson revolver
of the latest patern.
"See if he has not got another re
volver on him," said Veale, but the
boy replied that was all he could find.
SHERIFF R. R. YEALE.
"Get over on this side," shouted veale
to Moore, and the latter did as told.
During this conversation the Sheriff
kept his rifle leveled at Moore. Bon
zagni then picked up the shotgun that
Moore had dropped a.rjd found that it
was not only loaded, but that the ham
mer was up, showing that Moore was
ready for an emergency. The pack of
blankets was Riven back to the mur
derer, and he was made to walk ahead
and marched to town, Sheriff Veale
walking behind, with the Winchester
leveled at the criminal. The distance
from the point where Moore was cap
tured to town is about three miles, and
this Moore was compelled to cover at
the point of the rifle.
At the County Jail Moore had noth
ing to say. He was searched and a
great many articles taken from him.
While the searching was going on one
of the officers unrolled Moore's blan
kets and inside was a Colt's revolver,
the weapon of Police Officer Kilroy, and
by which identification was made al
most complete. When asked what he
was doing with so many revolvers
Moore had nothing to say. Among the
articles taken from Moore was a hunt
ing-case gold watch and chain, with
a compass fob attached to the charm.
On the outside of the case were en
graved the initials "L M." Besides
this there was a diamond ring, a lot of
aid coins, a gold stud, a razor, a flute,
a snuffbox, a shaving brush, a gold
penholder and pen, a bunch of keys, a
purse containing a lot of specimens of
ore, a pair of spectacles, a box of per
cussion caps, a pearl-handled knife, a
plain gold band ring, a gold ring with a
chain setting, a German silver tobacco
bt/x, a time-table of the Southern Pa
cific, a memorandum book, from the
cover of which the name of the owner
had been scratched, and a small bar of
silver with the name "1. Getchell" on it.
After Moore had been thoroughly
searched he told Sheriff Veale he
wished to communicate with a friend,
but when the officer brought him a
telegraph blank and pencil Moore said
he felt tired and would postpone the
matter until later. Moore said he felt
hungry and would like something to
cat. The Sheriff took him to a restau
rant, gave him a good meal, and then
brought him back to the jail. After
he was put into a cell he had a chill
and trembled like an aspen leaf.
Sheriff Veale telephoned to Sheriff
Getchell of Nevada City and told the
officer he had the murderer of Kilroy.
The first question that Getchell asked
was:
"Did he have a gold watch on him
with the initials '1.. M.' on it, and did
he have a Colts revolver with him?"
Getchell v.as informed that he had.
"That is our man, then," replied
Getchell. "See that he is well taken
care of, and do not under any circum
stances '"ow him to get away."
This evening a representative of The
Call had a talk with the murderer. He
stated that when arrested he -was on
his way to Oakland to see E. H. Moore
at 666 Claremont avenue, who is a rel
ative of his. Further than this the
man wouljl not say anything. He is
locked up in a steel tank at the jail
and a watc" of two men will be kept
over him during the night. The Sheriff
from Nevada City will arrive to-mor
row morning.

AT NEVADA CITY
NEVADA CITY, April 24.â This af
ternoon's press telegrams from Mar
tinez announcing the capture of Mur
derer Moore set the city wild with ex
citement. Crowds of men gathered on
the streets and congratulated them
selves and each other and commended
brave Sheriff Veale of Contra Costa
County for having rounded up the
notorious outlaw without firing a shot.
John and Joseph Â«Kilroy, the brothers
of the murdered policeman, threw ud
their hats and shouted with delieht at
the good news. With hundreds of
others, they had almost abandoned the
hope that the assassin ever could be
brought to justice. Most of the local

MAJOR THOMAS' PLEA
Insisted That the Colored Prisoner
Was Guiltless and Did His
Best to Save Him.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PALMETTO, Ga., April 24.â ThÂ»
body of Lige Strickland, the negro
preacher who was implicated J.n the
Oranford murder by Sam Hose, was
found swinging to the limb of a per
simmon tree within a mile and a quar
ter of this place early to-day. Before
death was allowed to end the sufferings
of the negro his ears were cut off and
the small finger of the left hand was
severed at the second joint. These
trophies were in Palmetto to-day. On
the chest of the netro was a scrap of
blood-stained paper, fastened with an
ordinary pin. On one side of this paper
was written:
"New York Journal: We nust pro
tect our ladies. 23, 99."
The other side of the paper contained
a warning to the negroes "le neigh
borhood. It read as follows:
"Beware, all darkies. You will be
treated the same way."
Before being finally lynchM Strick
land was riven - hance to confess to
the misdeeds of which the mob sup
posed him to be guilty, but he protest
ed his innocence until the end.
Three times the noose was placed
around his neck, and the negro was
drawn up and three times he was let
down, with warnings that death was
in store for him should he fail to con
fess his complicity in the Cranford
murder. Three times Â£ \:kland pro
claimed his torr*""" c, until, weary of
useless torturing, the mob pulled on
the rope and tied the end around the
slender trunk of the persimmon tree.
Not a shot was fired by the mob.
Strickland was strangled to death.
The lynching of Lige Strickland was
not accomplished without a desperate
effort tm the part of his employer to
save his life. The man who pleaded for
the negro is Major W. W. Thomas, an
ex-State Senator and one of the most
distingushed citizens of Coweta Coun
ty. He did all in his power to prevent
the lynching of the negro and did not
discontinue his efforts until he had
fieen assured by the leaders of the mob
that the negro would be taken to jail
at Fairburn. One mile fror- 1 where
this promise was made Lige Strickland
was hanged. The negro was a tenant
on the plantation of Major Thomas.
When Sam Hose, the murderer of Al
fred Cranford and the assailant of his
wife, made his confession immediately
prior to his burning, he implicated Lige
Strickland, Hose contending that he
had been offered money by Stricklajid
to kill Cranford. It was known posi
tively, however, that Hose had made
false statements in his last confession,
and many of those who aided in his
burning were disposed to disregard his
statement in regard to Strickland.
Late Sunday night about fifteen men
went to the plantation of Major Thomas
and took Strickland Trom the little
cabin in the woods that he called home,
leaving his wife and five children to
wail and weep over the fate they knew
was in store for the negro. Their cries
aroused Major Thomas, and that sturdy
old gentleman followed the lynchers in
his buggy, accompanied by his son, W.
M. Thomas, determined to save, if pos
sible, the life of his plantation darkey.
He overtook the lynchers with their
victim at Palmetto, and then ensued,
with only the moonlight to brighten
the faces of these grim men, the weird
est and most dramatic scene this sec
tion has ever known.
Lige Strickland was halted directly
opposite the telegraph office. The
noose was adjusted around his neck
and the end of the rope was thrown
over a tree. Strickland was told he
had a chance before dying to confess
his complicity in the crime. He re
plied:
"I have told you all I know, gentle
men. You can kill me if you wish, but
I know nothing more to tell."
The negro's life might have ended
then but for the arrival of Major
Thomas, who leaped from his buggy
and asked for a hearing. He asked
the crowd to give the negro a chance
for his life here on the streets of Pal
metto, and said:
"Gentlemen, this negro is innocent.
Hose said Lige had promised to give
him $20 to kill Alfred Cranford, and I
believe Lige has not had $20 since he
has been on my place. He has never
done any of you any harm, and now
I want you to promise me that you will
turn him over to the bailiff of this
town in order that he may be given a
hearing. I do not ask that you liber
ate him. Hold him, and if the courls
adjudge him guilty, hang him."
To this the mob replied that Strick
land had inflamed the negroes in the
neighborhood and had a bad reputa
tion, having been run away from East
Point several years ago.
Major Thomas reminded the mob
that the negro had voluntarily told ofÂ«>

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 25, 1899.
COGHLAN WILL
BE DISCIPLINED
First to Lose Command
of the Raleigh.
Frecial Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, April 24.â The Herald's
Washington correspondent sends the
following: Before the Raleigh leaves
New York to-morrow for Philadelphia
Secretary Long is expected by well
informed officers to take action which
will relieve Captain Joseph B. Coghlan
of his command.
In any event, it is said by these offi
cers, that unless Captain Coghlan's
letter satisfactorily explains the indis
creet speeches made by that officer he
will not he aboard the vessel when the ;
President goes to Philadelphia to par
ticipate in the festivities there.
Secretary Hay having removed all
chance of friction with Germany In
consequence of Captain Coghlan's re
marks by a frank explanation, the only
thing that remains to be done to finally
close the incident is to carry out the
promise made to the Berlin Govern
ment to discipline the Raleigh's com
mander. This will be done immediate
ly upon the receipt of the explanation
which Captain Coghlan has been called
upon to make.
Secretary Hay promptly closed the
diplomatic end of the incident during
a talk he had with Embassador yon
Hollenben, who called at the depart
ment to express the regret of his Gov
ernment that an officer of the United
States navy should go so far as to
discuss the conduct of Germany in the
manner that Captain Coghlan had done
both in the speech before the Union
League Club and that delivered at the
Army and Navy Club banquet Satur
day night. I have authority for the
statement that the Secretary replied
practically in this manner:
The remarks of Captain Coghlan, as j
reported in the press, were indiscreet
and improper in their character. They
were said to have been uttered at a din
ner party given by a private citizen in |
a private room of a club, and cculd
therefore have no public character |
whatever. The Navy Department :
would take such disciplinary measures
as might seem proper.
The German Embassador was natur
ally much gratified at this prompt ex
planation of Secretary Hay, and ac
quainted his Government by cable,
with the result of his call. The em
bassy officials find it difficult to detach
Captain Coghlan from his position as
an oflicer of the United States navy,
and therefore they insist he should
be disciplined. The Embassador is
willing to wait, however, for the. Navy
Department to take action, feeling as
sured that it will be of such a character â¢
as to fully atone for the offense.
I understand the complaint of the
German Embassa<lÂ«.r has been brought
by Secretary Hay to the attention of ,
both the President and Secretary Long.
The latter called at the White House
to-day and talked the matter over with
the President, but so far as could be
subsequently learned there has been no
change in the programme which the
Secretary outlined on Saturday even- ,
ing.
Before taking action the Secretary
will await ih-- receipt of the correct* d
version of Captain Coghlan's speech
from that officer, with a statement in
explanation. He hopes that Captain <
Coghlan mailed his answer this even-
Ing, so that he can get it before the
Raleigh sails to-morrow.
Naval officers scout the idea that j
Admiral Dewey requested Captain
Coghlan to lay the facts regarding the
conduct of the Germans before the
American people, as they say he is too
good a disciplinarian to even entertain
the idea. Admiral Dewey is not con- j
cerned in the matter in any way, as
Captain Coghlan is not now under his!
command, and he will not be consulted j
as to the action which the department j
will take.
The Navy Department has no present
intention of court-martialing Captain
Coghlan and will confine its action to
relieving him of his command, should j
his explanation be unsatisfactory, and
writing him a letter of censure.

SAY GERMAN OFFICIALS
BERLIN. April 24.â 1n official circles the

"The American Government has prompt

sonably ask. This alone, even If it were

the American Government is not in any
way identified with the utterances of a
solitary officer."

pel him to do. 1 '

and for that reason 1 have nothing to'

seem to have made. I cannot discuss the

TAPS SOUND FOR
VETERAN OGLESBY
LINCOLN, 111., April 24.â Former Governor Richard J. Oglesby fell dead
near this city at 1 o'clock this afternoon. He had been in ill-health for
some time, but the end was unexuected.
Governor Oglesby was born in Kentucky in 1824, and migrated to Illi
nois in 1836. He was admitted to the bar in 1845. He served in the Mexi
can and civil wars, rising to the rank of major general. He served two
terms as Governor of Illinois and served one term in the United States
Senate and retired to private life on his beautiful estate near Elkhart in
1866.

WILDE PROTESTS AGAINST
PROMOTION OF MILLER
WASHINGTON. April 21.-Captaln D.
F. Wilde, U. S. N., of the cruiser Boston
has protested against the promotion of-
Colonel Miller to be a brigadier general
as a reward for the capture of Iloilo. It
appears that this capture was effected
entirely by naval forces under the com
mand of Captain Wilde, and that it was
not until after the capture had been ef
fected that the place was turned over to
Colonel Miller, who up to that time had
nothing to do with the capture. This ac
tion is indorsed by Admiral Dewey.
Wilde's protest comes too late to be ef
fective, as Colonel Miller was promoted
brigadier general February 15, in recogni
tion of his services In the capture of Iloilo,
and was retired in that grade March 27
by operation of law on account of agi
He was in command of the expedition Bent
from Manila for the capture of Iloilo, and
the cruisers Boston ami Petrel wore sent
with him to render assistance. While t he
vessels of theÂ» xptdition were lying on" the
port the natives began to strengthen their
defenses and fired several shots at the Pe
trel, which vessel was anchored nearest
the shore. The commander of that same
warship asked and received permission of
Captain Wilde of the Boston to respond.
At this time, according to naval ad
vices, General Miller's army transports
were far out in the roadstead. Without
communicating with General Miller, the
two warships opened on the shore batter
ies and soon Bllenced them. Lieutenant
Nlblack "f the Boston landed with a bat
talion of marines and sailors and took
possession of the town. They captured
the insurgent flags on the Governor's
house and on the batteries and turned the
city over to General Miller on his arrival,
an hour or so later. They kept the cap
tured flags, however, in spite of General
Miller's demand for them. This is the
naval story of the battle of Iloilo.
The official report of that engagement
first received in Washington was one by
General Miller to the war Department
and siniply announced the surrender of
that city to the American forces under
his command, without going into partlcu
lars. Acting upon this report and in view
of the fact that Genera! Miller had but a
few more weeks to serve on the active
list, the President appointed him to a va
cancy Jhen existing in the list of briga
dier generals. He is now on his way
home from Manila and is expected to
reach San Francisco in a few days.

REJECTED LOVER
TAKES HIS LIFE
Suicide of H. E. Petersen
at Salinas.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SALINAS, April 24.â Another of those
sad casts wherein a young man des
perately in love -with a pretty maiden
of fickle disposition ends life rather
than be subjected to the taunts of the
gossiping public for having been jilted
culminated in this city this evening,
when H. K. Petersen shot himself in
the head with a revolver and died soon
afterward.
For a long time it has been known
that Peteraen, who was 28 years old,
has been attentive to Miss Bertha Mar
tin, a Watsonville young lady promi
nent in society circles of that city, and
they have lung been regarded as be
trc-thed. This love affair commenced
several years ago and up to only a few
months ago Petersen lived in the town
which was the home of his affianced.
He was employed by the Spreckels
Company, had a fine position, had ac
cumulated quite a little wealth and
was very devoted to Miss Martin.
Several months ago, so the story
goes, Petersen having been removed to
the office of the company near this city,
Miss Martin found another man who
suited her fancy, and as a result Peter
sen did not receive letters as frequently
as he wished. Last Saturday night he
and a companion drove over to Watson
ville to remain over Sunday with rela
tives. W. Wood, who accompanied
Petersen, says the young man seemed
very happy on the trip going over, but
on the return it was different. During
the drive to this city Petersen hinted
there had been a quarrel.
This afternoon at 5 o'clock the pro
prietor of the Spreckels Hotel heard the
report of a revolver in the room of
Petersen. He entered and found the
young man lying on the bed, the blood
streaming from a wound in his head.
A faint cry of "I did it for her," and
Petersen was dead. A note on the
table was found, signed by Peteraen
and addressed to his brother, stating
that he was tired of life.
GREAT BRITAIN'S ACTION
IS SOMEWHAT PECULIAR
While Abrogating the Clayton-Bul
wer Treaty, It Tries to Control
Nicaraguan Transporta
tion Lines.
NEW YORK, April 24.â A Washington
special to the Herald says: President
Iglesius of Costa Rica called at the State
Department this morning and conferred
with Secretary Hay. The Secretary has
impressed upon the Costa Ricans that the
Presideni does not deem it expedient, in
view of the act of Congress directing fur
ther investigation of the isthrniam canal
routes, to construct the Nicaragua canal.
Although Great Britain is willing: to re
linquish her rights to share in the Nica
raguan canal by abrogating the Clayton-
Bolwer treaty in return for a quid pro
quo from the United States, it is apparent
to tile officials of the State Department
that she is taking measures to provide
transportation facilities acroÃS Nicaragua
for her own trade.
No official confirmation has been re
ceived of the dispatch from Managua say
ing President Zelaya had granted an op
tion to the British Consul at Managua or
to any company he may organize to pur
chase the railroads and steamboats of
Nicaragua, with workshops appertaining
to them, for the sum of 6,500,000 pesos
(silver), but the details of the agreement
are expected by the State Department.
It is considered very significant that
the representative of Great Britain should
take measures for placing under British
control transportation facilities in Nica
ragua while negotiations are in progress
between the United States and Great
Britain for the abrogation of the Clayton-
Bulwer treaty and with it the surrender
of all the latter's rights in the Nicaragua
canal.

BIG FISH CAUGHT
IN FEDERAL NET
Prominent Counterfeit
ers in the Toils.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PHILADELPHIA, April 24.â Former
United States District Attorney Ellery
P. Ingham of this city and Deputy
Internal Revenue Collector Samuel B.
Downey of Lancaster were arrested by
secret service officers to-day on the
charge of aiding the Lancaster coun
terfeiters in their efforts to flood the
country with bogus treasury notes and
internal revenue tobacco stamps. The
arrest of Ingham created a great sen
sation on account of his prominence
and his former connection with the
' Government as United States Attorney
for the eastern district of Pennsylva
nia. Those who are now under arrest
charged with complicity in the great
plot are: Ellery H. Ingham, ex-United
j States District Attorney, of this city;
, Henry K. Newitt, ex- Assistant United
; States District Attorney, of this city:
Samuel B. Downey, Deputy Internal
Revenue Collector of the Ninth Inter
ned Revenue District of Pennsylvania,
lof Lancaster, Pa. ; Williams Jacobs of
Lancaster, William F. Kendig of Lan
caster; Thomas Burns, Kendig's fore
man; Arthur Taylor, expert engraver.
of this city, and Baldwin S. Bredell,
expert engraver and mechanic, of this.
| city.
Ever since the conspiracy was un
j earthed last Thursday the air has been
thick with rumors of further arrests.
John E. Wilkie, chief of the secret
service division of the United States
Treasury, and United States District
Attorney James Beck, who is Ingham's
immediate successor, were in Lancas
ter yesterday, accompanied by Captain
W. J. Burns, of the secret service.
With the information gathered at
Lancaster, Mr. Beck early to-day ap
peared before United States Commis
sioner. Edmunds and swore out war
rants for the arrest of Ingham and
Downey. Chief Wilkie and Captain
Burns went to Lancaster this after
noon and arrested Downey, while Dep
uty United States Marshal Foster took
Ingham into custody at the latter^
law office. Mr. Ingham was not sur
prised.
The ex-District Attorney was imme
diately taken before Commissioner Ed
munds and formally charged with con
spiring with Harvey Newitt, his law
partner, "'to promise, offer and give"
the sum of $6000 in monthly payments
of $500 each to William J. McManus of
Philadelphia, a secret service officer,
who was working up the case against
the five men charged with counterfeit
ing revenue stamps and the $100 silver
certificates. Ingham waived a hearing
and was held in $10,000 bail for a fur
ther hearing on Thursday morning.
Newitt's hearing, which had been fixed
for to-morrow, was changed so that
i both men can be arraigned together.
i Joseph R. Grundy of Bristol, Pa.,
I Newitt's bondsman, went Ingham's
ball.
Ellery P. Ingham has been prominent
;as a Republican in State politics. He
; was appointed United States Attorney
I for the eastern district of Pennsylva
| nla by President Harrison, but was
succeeded at the end of his term by
James M. Beck, who received his ap
pointment from President Cleveland.
During the last few years Mr. Ingham
â  has been a prominent figure at State
' conventions, as an adherent of ex-Sen
, ator Quay.
Samuel B. Downey, the deputy reve
j nue collector, was in consultation with
; his attorneys at Lancaster when Cap
j tain Burns took him in custody. He
was unable to give the $10,000 bail fixed
by United States Commissioner Mont
gomery, and was brought to Philadel- j
phia and lodged in the County Jail.
The Secret Service officers say that
Downey received about $1000 from Ja
cobs and Kendig for furnishing them
with information regarding the move
ments of Government detectives.
When interviewed to-night Chief
Wilkie spoke guardedly as to the evi
dence he expects to produce against
the men arrested to-day. He said,
however, that Downey had practically
solicited bribes from the counterfeiters,
! having told Jacobs that he was in fln
i anclal trouble and asked him for ad
vice. Jacobs first loaned him $100, and
later paid him larger sums. Dowmy
promising to keep Jacobs informed as
to what the Government officials were
doing. â¢
In regard to the arrest of Ingham,
Chief Wilkie would not say much.
"All I can say is this." he said, "that
Jacobs and the rest of the gang wanted
to get inside information from the Se
cret Service men in this city. Ingham
and Newitt were formerly United
States attorneys and. knew much of the
inside workings of the Government.
Jacobs knew this and approached them
cautiously, but he finally got there. To
day's arrests are the last that will be
made in connection with the case, un
less there is more in it than we know
now. lam perfectly satisfied with the
result of our year's work. In results
it was the greatest capture ever made
by the Secret Service. Taylor and
Bredell had already put into circula
tion bogus $100 treasury notes to the
amount of $10,000, and Jacobs and Ken
dip: had placed counterfeit stamps on
boxes containing between 40,000,000
and 50,000,000 cigars. The loss to the
Government from the cigar stamps
alone will amount to about $140,000.
CAPTAIN DREYFUS WILL
NEVER BE LIBERATED
LONDON. April 25.â David Christie
Murray publishes this morning the state
ment that he has learned from trust
worthy sources in Paris that the superior
council of war, including General de
Negxier, General Zurlinden. General Gio
vanninelli, General Duchcsne and Gen
oral Jemont, its vice president, none of
whom, except perhaps General Zurlinden.
has heretofore been implicated in the
Dreyfus affair, has decided that, under
no circumstances whatever, shall Dreyfus
be liberated.
He understands also, from the same
source, that negotiations are now pro
ceeding between the Intelligence Bureau
and the judges of the Court of Cassation
to secure a rejection of the request for
revision and that the judges who favor
revision are being subjected to terrible in
timidation. More than this, he learns
thnt everything has been arranged for a
sham trial of Colonel Ploquart, who ia to
be degraded and pentencÂ«d to imprison
ment for ten years.

DEMAND RECOGNITION
OF A MINERS' UNION
"WARNER, Idaho, . April 24.â Another
labor strike was started here last night
when the "Wardner Miners' Union de
manded that the Bunker Hill and Sulli
van Company, employing 360 men. ad
vance wages to $3 50 ail around and rec
ognize the union. The company is pay
ing $3 and $2 50 and has not recognized the
union, since the great strike of 1892. The
managers replied that they would never
recognize the union; that rather than do
so they would shut down the mine for
twenty years; but they are willing to ad
vance wages to $3 50 for miners and 13
for car men and shov lera.
The union, which has been working Its
men into the mine for a long time, claims
that 800 men came out on its call last
night. The company claims that it has
lost only 120.
A similar demand was made on the Em
pire State Idaho Company, formerly the
Last Chance. Charles Sweeney, one of the
owners, arrived to-day from Spokane and
ordered the mill and the mine closed. He
will discuss matters with the union to
morrow. There have been no disorders,
but many apprehend there will be.

After an early luncheon to-day the Na
tives, accompanied by the Fifth Regi
ment band of San Jose, boarded the
dummy line trains to visit Sprockets and
be shown over the great sugar factory.
At the entrance to the factory grounds
they were met by Superintendent W. C.
Waters and a corps of guides, and all
climbed about amid the ponderous ma
chinery from the ground floor to the top
iof the enormous building. Everything
was interesting, not only because of the
'â  size of the plant and the monetary outlay
represented, but because of the beauty of
1 the machinery and the skill of the me
chanics who constructed so vast and
complicated a plant. The building and
its equipments are yet incompleted, and
those among the Natives who under
stand machinery were diverted by watch
ing the small army of workmen em
ployed about the place. Each visitor was
handed a card with the following sta
tistics printed on It as a souvenir of the
afternoon:

SHOOTING AFFRAY
NEAR PASO ROBLES
PASO ROBL.ES, April 24.â William
Trancing, who lives in the Red Hills, near
the town of Shandon, yesterday shot a
man named Simpson, son of the late Dr.
Simpson of San Francisco, twice through
the side Drs. Hughes and Hall attended
the wounded man and extracted the bul
lets. Lancing was at one time a member
of the Board of Education of San Fran
cisco and is quite prominent in politics.
No arrests have been made.
WILLIAM HOLLIETT KILLED.
SONOMA, April 24.â Engineer William
Holllett met an accidental death at the
electric light power house this afternoon.
Holliett suddenly disappeared and a
search of the premises was instituted.
The body of the unfortunate man was dis
covered in the water beneath the gasoline
tank, where he had evidently been en
gaged in drawing off gasoline preparatory
to starting up for the night. It is sup
posed the young man became overcome
by the gases and fell head foremost into
the pit beneath the tanks, his head strik
ing on the timbers. His neck was broken.
Young Holliett had been In charge of
the plant only a week. He came here
from San Francisco and was yesterday
visited by his brother from that city. He
was a single* man and aged about 20
years.
A CARDINAL
IN CURIA MAY
BE CHOSEN
New Catholic Official Likely
to Represent the United
States.
ROME HIS RESIDENCE
Archbishop Keane Has Great Popu
larity at the Vatican and Is
Mentioned for Elevation.
t
Special Dispatch to The <'ai!.
NEW FORK, April 24.â Private ad
vices from Rome Indicate the proba
bility of important changes in the of
ficial relations of the Catholic church
here with the Vatican. A Cardinal in
curia to represent the United States
may be appointed.
Mgr. Martinelli is mentioned as the
coming nuncio to Paris.
A Cardinal from South America is
considered a certainty.
Cardinals in curia are prelates who
form the in .ediate entourage of the
Pope's administration. They are in
vested with such authority as enables
them to be heard directly on affairs of
the nations th represent. These
Cardinals always reside in Rome.
They are important functionaries in
the diplomatic intercourse of the Vat
ican. European powers are very jeal
ous and eager about their selection.
Of course, as the United States has
no official political connection with the
Vatican, similar relations could not be
established for the American republic,
but it is said the authorities in Wash
ington have been indirectly approached
in the matter. Churchmen who will
talk about the subject hint that when
Archbishop Ireland and Archbishop
Keane return here something more
denufto will be available on this point.
Archbishop Keane returns to take up
the work of collecting indorsements for
the Catholic University at Washington,
of which he was the first rector. His
name, of course, is mentioned in con- '
nection with the nomination of Cardi- <
nal in curia. His great popularity in |
Rome among Vatican officials would
make his elevation acceptable. What
opposition might be made on this side)
of tho Atlantic will have to be devel- I
oped lat^r. If his personal wishes are '
consulted, the Archbishop, his intimate j
friends say, would rather stay on this I
sid< of the ocean, but he is so valuable i
an adjunct to the Papal court that j
while the present Pope lives he will !
probably be kept abroad.
To several of the Cardinals who are i
mentioned as likely to succeed Leu I
XIII he is even more acceptable, and j
I added honors are sure to follow the I
elevation of either of them to the Papal ;
throne. The nomination may also be ,
hastened by a change in the Apostolic
delegation at Washington.
The recent death of the nuncio to
Paris leaves a vacancy at that impor
tant post. It is current gossip that
Mgr. Martinelli may be transferred
from Washington to Paris as nuncio, \
thus receiving a reward for his work j
here and prefacing his early elevation
to the sacred college, in which his
brother, the late Cardinal Thomas |
Martinelli, was long one of the Pope's j
most active and helpful friends. Such j
a change would necessitate a readjust- !
ment of the official machinery of the !
church all along the line. The coming
consistory will have an important
bearing on the whole subject.

years. Mayor Phelan held at

longer before the board, its
action in naming a rate of
$1 10 being final and not sub
ject to revision. Hence the
bills presented to consumers
for the month of April should
be at the reduced rate.

sinners and the company. The Super- ',

EPISCOPAL CONVOCATION.
Important Session of the Clergy of
This City and Surrounding
Towns to Be Held.
The spring meeting of the convocation
of the Episcopal church will be held in St.
John's Church, corner Fifteenth and Val
encia, on the 9th of next month. The i
Initial serviqe will he held at 10 o'clock on
thai day and will consist of the celebra
tion of 'the holy communion. A sermon
will be preached by the Rev. H. W. L.
O'Rorke, chaplain of the Seamen's Insti
tute. The regular business of the convo
cation will then be presented. An attempt
will be made to hear the reports of the \
missionaries of this district before the ad
journment for lunch. The ministers will
again convene at 2 o'clock in the after
noon. During the session the matter of
arranging for the distribution of the mis
sionary funds among the parishes and
missions will be taken up. Reports will
also be heard from the various charitable
institutions. Among the organizations
named are St. Luke's Hospital, Maria Kip
Orphanage, "Id Ladies' Home and the
Sheltering Arms. Immediately after the
ntatlon of these reports discussion
will be had on them.
The evening session will be given up to
the consideration of the Sunday school
work of the convocation. Some further
discussion oil the organization of the
diocesan Sunday school institute will be
had, and it is also hoped that an address
will be delivered by Rev. Hamilton Lee of
Martinez on the "Needs and Difficulty s of
Sunday School Work in the Country."
HisV.ni> Nichols will preside during the
session. During his absence the Rev. E.
J. Lion, rector of St. Stephen's Church,
will be in the chair. It is expected that
all the Episcopal clergy of this city and
of the bay cities will be in attendance.
Dr. M. O. WllÃon, secretary of the convo
cation, is arranging the details for the
coming meeting, which promises to be one
of the most important ones of the year

THE RAILROAD YOKE
His Conference With W. F. Herrin
Creates Commotion at
the Palace.
Friends of the Cbief Executive Advised to Instruct
Him in the Duties and Responsibilities of
His High Office in the State.
THE flying visit of Governor Gage
to San Francisco has occasioned
â widespread comment in this
city. Men of affairs were as
tounded that the chief executive
of the State would so far forget the
dignity of his office, and whatever self
i respect he may possess, as willingly
; and brazenly to submit himself pub
; licly to the domination of an influence
[ that is notorious in its corruption
iof public men. When Governor
! Gape, hurriedly and secretly, en
| tered the Palace Hotel on Sunday af
i ternoon to meet William F. Herrin of
the Southern Pacific Company it was
impossible to misconstrue the purpose
of the Governor's mission.
William F. Herrin occupies no official
station in California. He is not auali
' fied either by authority or association
to be an adviser of the chief executive
of California. The chief of the law de
partment of the Southern Pacific Com
pany is hired by a powerful corpora
tion to manipulate public affairs and
influence public men. His advice is
sought by public men in secret and by
stealth, and Governor Gape stands ar
raigned as the first Governor of Cali
fornia who has flown in the face r.f
decent public opinion and prostrai >d
himself before the representative and
agent of a corporation whose name is
a synonym for evil.
It is the boast of Governor Gape that
before he was elected Governt/r of Cali
fornia he never held public office. He
may be ignorant therefore of the policy
of public men. He should learn that a
-nor (if California does not tran-
Bact public business as a hunted fugi
tive. He should know that associated
with his high office is a dignity and a
responsibility to the people which his
predecessors have honored and ob
served. His friends should inform him
that a Governor of California who has
putation to lose does not take a
gambling blackleg for a dictator and a
railroad employe for a counselor.
Governor c,;ige should be told that
the Chief Executive of this State does
not welcome aa a guard of honor in a
public hotel a horde of gamblers,
thieves, political roustabouts and ras-
I;. Governors of California have not
n in the habit of having such men
their train. They do not slip by
ilth into a public place to confer
NEW CITY TRUSTEES
TAKE THEIR OFFICE
FORDERER FORCED TO YIELD
THE GAVEL TO BRICK.
The Trustees Promise to Try and
Conduct the City Affairs Within
the Dollar Limit.
AX.AMEDA, April 24.â The new Hoard
of City Trustees took office this evening.
Joseph Forderer, who >ias been chairman
for the past six years, was forced to re
tire and was succeeded by John G. Brick.
George H. Miller nominated Brick and in.
doing so said:
"1 am sorry that Mr. Forderer cannot
be continued as chairman. The vote by
which he was returned to this board
shows that the people approve his course,
but political changes make it necessary
to place a new man in the chair, and I
hope he will get the unanimous vote of
the members and save him the humilia
tion of being forced to vote for him
self."
Mr. Forderer seconded the nomination
of Mx. Brick. He said:
"I was told by the opposition that it
was an absolute political necessity for me
to step down and out and that none of
the reform measures promised by the
party in control could be carried nut with
me in the chair. I am n<>t an obstruc
tionist and proposes to give the other fel
lows fvil! swing."
Mr. Crick stated that no one would he
discharged from the city's employ who
did his duty. No changes were contem
plated, be said, except in the electric
fight department, which is to be conduct
ed "M more economical plans. The new
Trustees will try to conduct the city af
fairs within the dollar limit. Mr. Brick
appointed the following committees:
Judiciaryâ Brick. Mackie.
Auditing and Financeâ Mackie. Sehulte.
Lights, Water, Printing and Advertis
ingâ Sehulte, Mackie.
Fire, Police and Public BuildingsâFor
derer, Miller.
Streets. Sewers, Wharves and Public
Healthâ Miller and Forderer.
â  Care was taken not to appoint members
of the maiority and minority on the Bame
committees. The old Board of Health was
reappolnted.

SANTA CLARA AHEAD.
The College Nine Handsomely De
feated the Cracks of the State
Normal School.
A rattling good baseball game was
played last Saturday at Santa Clara be
tween the nine representing the State
Normal School and the Santa Clara ('ol
lege team. The Santa Clara boys upheld
their reputation as champion athletes and
handsomely defeated their opponents by a
score of 5 to 1.
The Normal School boys are not satis
fled with their defeat, and it is expected
that a return match will Boon be played
on the old grounds. The Santa Clara
baseball team Is regarded by the players
of the country as being the strongest
amateur nine in the State.
CALLED TO ST. LUKES.
Rev. D. C. Garrett of Portland Will
Advise the Vestry as to
His Decision.
It is more than probable that the Rev.
D. C. Garrett of Trinity Episcopal
Church, Oregon, may be the next pastor
of St. l.nkg's Church in this city. He
preached hero twice on Sunday and made
a most favorable impression. He is a fine
orator ;m<l possesses a magnetic deliv
ery. At a meeting of the vestry of St.
Luke's, held yesterday in the office of A.
N Drown. 104 Sutter street. Rev. Mr. Gar
rett appeared and discussed the advisa
bility- of his accepting the call. He in
formed the vestry that he would advise
them as to his decision. He left last night
for his home in Portland. This Is the
second time that he has been called to
this city.
RECOVERED HER PIN.
' Mrs. Rose Finds Her Diamond Cres
cent Which She Lost.
Mrs. George Rose, wife of the hook
makpr, recently lost a diamond crescent,
: which she valued at $500. She offered a
i reward of $100 for the recovery of the lost
' grem. Several days ago a man named
I Hirsch, who conducts a dry goods store
at 221 Montgomery avenue, visited her
house and offered to return the crescent if
she gave him $150.
Mrs. Rose visited police headquarters
and informed Captain Bohen of the
demand made by Hirsch. She explained
that she was willing to give him the
amount offered, but refused to pay the
exorbitant demand of Hirsch.
Captain Bohen Instructed Mrs. Rose to
visit Hirsch's store and ask to be allowed
to see the pin. He showed it to Mrs.
Rose, who, acting under instructions from
! Detectives Dinap and Tom Gibson, who
; were standing on the opposite side of the
; street. Bhe took it tn her hand, and un
i der th^ pretense that the light was dim in
the store, stepped outside.
Just as she did the detectives stepped
up to her and took possession of the pin.
Hirsch showed a disposition to resent the
Interference of the detectives, but finally
consented to allow the crescent to be
placed in the hands of the property clerk
of the Police Department until the matter
is adjusted In the Police Court.
Hirsch claims be bought the pin from
a man named Eckstein for $40. The latter
claimed he had found it on Larkin street,
near Sutter.

WILL SETTLE
THE SAMOAN
DIFFICULTY
High Commissioners
Arrive.
HAVE FULL AUTHORITY TO ACT
WILL PROCEED TO APIA ON
TRANSPORT BADGER.
Commission Will Supersede Existing
Government of the Islands and
Has Power to Modify
Berlin Treaty.
The members of the Samoan High Com
mission arrived in this city last night
from Washington and took quarters at
the Palace. The commission consists of
Bartlett Tripp of Yankton, S. D., the
American representative, E. Elliott, the
British member, and Baron Sternburg,
â who will look after Germany's interests.
They will proceed to Samoa on the trans
port Badger, which sails to-morrow. Each
CommiHsioner carries the individual in
structions of his Government, and last
night Mr. Tripp summed them up sub
stantially as follows:
The commission is given full authority
to carry on a provisional government for
Samoa. This Government may be stable
or temporary, as the conditions may war
rant. It is provided that the duties of all
those exercising authority in Samoa,
either under the treaty of Berlin or in
the service of either of the Governments,
Fhail be suspended in order that the High
Commission may direct the affairs of the
islands undisturbed. In doing this the
Instructions carefully prescribe the pres
ervation of order and the security of life
and property.
The commission will make a report upon
the recent collisions and the causes lead-
In?? up to them: the administration of the
islands, the rights of the several claim
ants to the kingship and also as to what
changes arc desirable in the methods pro
vided by the treaty of Berlin. The final
Bgrcr m<nt calls for unanimity of action,
which is to be conclusive, except when
it suspends a provision of the treaty of
Berlin. In that case a provision of the
treaty may be suspended by unanimous
agreement of the members, but the sus
pension is not final until the three Gov
ernments approve or disapprove.
Mr. Tripp has been Judge of the Su
preme Court of South Dakota, was Min
ister to Austria under President Cleve
land and is an experienced diplomat.
Baron Bternburg has been first secretary
of the German embassy, and .Mr. Elliot
was second secretary of the English em
bassy to lie United States. Both gentle
men have also been connected with Ori
ental legations and are likewise well
versed in the art of diplomacy.
In speaking of the affair, Judge Tripp
said:
"It is our desire to avoid International
complications in the settlement of the
problem. The three members are in strict
accord on the main issues. We realize
that It is a delicate question, for," he
added significantly, "grave consequences
have ensued for a smaller cause than the
one at issue."

AROUND THE
CORRIDORS
F. T. Sutherland, a mine owner of
Placer, Is at the Palace.
F. H. Smythe. foreman of the Langtry
ranch, is a guest at the Russ.
W. M. Garland, a prominent real estate
operator of Los Angeles, is registered at
the Palace.
Thomas H. Thompson, a fruit grower of
Tulare, and J. M. Wilmans, a stock
raiser of Newman, are among the arrivals
at the Lick.
H. H. Blood, a mining man of Virginia
City, New ; Truman Coffin, an attorney
of Carson, and J. D. Fairchild, a mer
chant of Yreka, are registered at the
Grand.
Adolph Fleishman of the wholesale
fruit firm of Loeb, Fleishman & Co. of
Los Angeles is in the city for a few days
and is staying at the California.
H. C. Whitney, chief clerk of the freight
department of the Chicago, West Michi
gan and Detroit Railway and the Grand
Rapids and Western Railroad, is in the
city for a brief stay.
Albert Ross, the novelist, is one of the
passengers booked for Honolulu on the
Coptic, which sails next Friday. He is
on a two years' trip around the world
and has been in California for four
months gathering materials for a story.
John A. Gill, Pacific Coast freight agent
of the New York Central lines, has re
turned from his trip to Oregon and Puget
Sound points. In discussing the outlook
for the Northwestern section, Mr. Gill
said: "Oregon alone will ship over thirty
million pounds of wool this year. This
will 'include five million pounds left over
from last year's crop. A beet sugar fac
tory Is about to be erected at Waverley,
Wash., to cost $500,000. The hop prospects
for Oregon and Washington are unusually
fine. The Pad! Coast prospects are
generally brighter than they ever were in
every branch of trade."

BATHOS UNABLE
TO MAKE GOLDEN
RULE LIMBER UP
Candelaria Stake No
Race at All.
MUDDY GOING AT OAKLAND
MARY BLACK BEATEN IN A SEN
Â« SATIONAL FINISH.
Rosinante Came From the Clouds and
â Satsuma in Bare Fettle.
Amasa Beaten by Harry
Thoburn.
Golden Rule, the enterprising son of
Imp. Golden Garter and Lucille Murphy,
added the Candelaria handicap to his
string of victories at Oakland yesterday.
;An indifferent field of low class two
year-olds opposed him, and he handled
his 127 pounds over the five-furlong run
without effort. Bathos alone was con
ceded a chance to beat the favorite, but
Ferguson gave the Daly youngster the
Klondike face at the start, and the
ground thus lost never made him trouble
! some. The muddy going gave Golden
; Rule no trouble, for he won as he wished
in 1:06. Bathos led Racetto out two
j lengths for the place.
Showery weather and the difficulty of
j picking winners over a muddy course kept
many away from the track. Favorites
about held their own by winning three \
events.
The most sensational event of the after
noon was the mile and a furlong run, for
which Mary Black ruled a decided favor
ite. Shouldering John ReifC and 101
pounds she started off at a heart-break
ing pace, actually losing her field the first
mile, which was reeled off in 1:42%. It
was too warm, for the mare began Stop
! ping the last part, and Rosinante, with
! Jenkins in the saddle, dropped from the
I clouds, winning by a neck.
Harry Thoburn's well-known iiking for
slushy going sent him to the post a slight
favorite over Amasa and Sly for the open
ing sprint. Basslnger had the leg up, and
trailing Amasa into the stretch, passed
the latter at the paddock, winning ridden
out by a couple of lengths. Sly finished
a close third.
Bald-faced Satsuma came out fresh and
frisky as a two-year-old, taking the nolle
purse run in a common canter. A pro
nounced favorite in the betting ring.
Rosormonde led him to the stretch, where
Dr. Rowel's horse took the lead, and it
was all over. Lost Girl managed to get
the show from San Venado.
A poor start ruined the chances of
Plan, the 7 to 10 favorite for the mile and
a sixteenth selling number. After get
. ting away poorly, Ruiz persevered with
; the gelding, but was late in getting up,
and met defeat by a head from Widow
Jones, which led from the jump.
Wyoming went to the post first choice i
! for the seven-furlong event, which closed
the day's racing, but was beaten in hol
low fashion by Imperious, the second
choice.
Track Notes.
The stable of Dr. Rowell was very suc
cessful, capturing three purses. It waa
the first time In a month that the doctor's

ThjE CARTOON AS (\ MORfIL WEAPON.
IZ^OR the anti-cartoon law which has now gone
into effect the press of the State almost uni
versally holds The Call responsible. A car
toon, "Johnson, the penman," published in this paper
during the session of the Legislature is said to have
been the spur which made the galled jade of official
corruption wince and buck to the extent of enacting
the law.
1: this be true. The Call has no regrets for its
course; no apologies to offer. On the contrary, it
cites that very fact as a convincing evidence of the
usefulness of the cartoon to the press and to the pub
lic, a proof of its effectiveness as a moral weapon, a
demonstration of its potency to so hold the mirror
up to rascality and folly that the guilty offender
biushes at his depravity and "weeps to find it fame."
For years the ablest writers and the ablest orators
of California have employed every weapon of argu
ment, reason, satire and invective to pierce the thick
hide of Johnson's self-conceit and shameless audac
ity, to expose his true nature so clearly that not only
would it be plain to all the world, but that even he,
!;iind as he is in his besotted egotism, might see him
self as others see him and realize his moral degrada
tion.
All these efforts of tongue and pen were vain. It
was in vain the eloquent Edgerton, that master of
an oratory whose invective burned and flamed like
tire, assailed the impudence of Johnson; it was in
vain McClatchy of the Bee, one of the most virile
and vitriolic writers who ever essayed to drive cor
ruption from politics, opposed his pretensions and
exposed his record. In vain many another orator
and many another writer tried each in turn to make
him feel in his heart the -sting of public scorn. Ail
words of tongue and pen were vain; they were futile;
they had no effect.
Then The Call published its cartoon. Grove John
son was pictured visibly so that the eye could see
him as he is. With that pictured page before them
the people saw what his record is and what he is.
lie himself saw himself, and knew at last how the
people sec him. and the hide of his egotism was
pierred, his very impudence was shamed into re
morse; he broke down and wept.
An intelligent public can demand no stronger or
more convincing proof of the effectiveness of the"
cartoon as a moral weapon. The pencil of the skilled
artist succeeds where all words fail. It carries con
viction not only to the public, but to the mind of
rascality itself. It is the most potent means which
virtue has to expose vice, folly, fraud and hypocrisy
and make their hideousness so clear that even those
who practice them shrink from them in dismay.
A free press charged with the duty of exposing
evil and driving from high places all who practice
it cannot surrendar this effective weapon at the com- |
mand of malicious rascality. The anti-cartoon law
enacted in a moment of fright and malice is uncon

moral weapon and will not lay it down so long as
there is a battle to be fought for virtue and honesty
in California.

WHAT IS TREASON?
AMERICANS have a profound respect for their
Government as created and supported by them.
This generates in the feeling that if the prin
ciples of the constitution are enforced the Govern
ment compels respect for the popular rights which are
secured by law.
The anti-imperialist sentiment of the country has
its origin in this regard of the people for the prin
ciples of their Government, and their fear that those
principles may Sustain a deathly shock by the exer
cise of powers beyond our continental borders which
the constitution docs not permit to the Government
within those borders.
When a major general in the United States army
said last January that it makes no difference if im
perialism is a violation of the constitution, for the
republic has outgrown that instrument and it is no
longer worth discussing, he was not rebuked by the
Government, nor was his destructive utterance dis
avowed by imperialists. Others, prominent in civil
life, have entered upon a regular campaign, as they
declare, to teach the people the absurdity of the
Declaration of Independence and the nonsense of the
statement therein that Governments derive their just
powers from the consent of the governed.
The course followed on these two lines has seemed
to a large number of thoughtful people as leading to
the destruction of our insiitutions.
The effect of the policy it is intended to justify has
developed quite rapidly. Within ten day. an impe-ial
i ist organ in this city has declared that we must pro-
I ccc : without mercy to the suhdtiing of the rebellion
S of "our subjects" in the Philippines, and the term
"subjects" hi this country in the same connection has
appeared twice since in news dispatches in which the
matter has been discussed.
There are men and patriots in the country who be
lieve that "subjects" and "citizens" cannot long exist
under a Government which derives all of its powers,
| and all of its sovereignty, and all of its jurisdiction
â¢ from the constitution. They believe that the tendency
of events will be to make all men under that sover
eignty "subjects" or Filipinos "citizens," and they re
gard either as disastrous to our institutions.
In order to discredit these sentiments, which a year
ago were universally held by the American people, it
has become necessary to stamp them as treason to the
Government, and this is being done by charging the
anti-imperialists with sending dispatches to troops in
the Philippines, advising against their re-enlistment in
the hard service in which they have borne themselves
so gallantly for the last two months. It is probably true
that parents of volunteers whose enlistment has ex
pired have exhorted them to come home, but it is
probably untrue that other parties, not in the pa
rental relation to volunteers nor moved by domestic
affection, have used the cable or the mail for any such
purpose. The last form of the story that cablegrams
have been sent signed "committee" is still less prob
able, for any one with intelligence and resources
enough to semi lengthy cablegrams knows that the
cable to the Philippines i.> in possession of our mili
tary authorities and that such dispatches \s<-uld never
reach the men in the camps or the field. The state
ment has been propagated that these dispatches have
changed the spirit of the volunteers and that they no
longer desire to re-enlist, and at the same time it is
.red that the dispatches were seized and held by
the military authorities. The two statements are in
consistent and destroy each other.
The censorship at Manila is unleakably strict, and
even as public news nothing passes that is not agree
able to the military censor.
As far as developed, the anti-imperial sentiment i-i
this country is unaffected by war or peace in the Phil
ippines. The complete pacification of the islands to- j
morrow would in no way change it. It is a sentiment
held from the point of view of our welfare at home
and arises in a desire lor the perpetuity of our free
institutions and the peaceful development of our re
sources by a free people.
In its essence this sentiment is nut partisan. It
has no sympathy with Mr. Bryan's labored effort to
make politics out of it. With Senator Morgan, the
Democratic leader of the Senate, advocating expan
sion, standing for the killing of all the Samoans and
Filipinos as a necessary precedent to the introductioi
of civilization, and Sep.;;: or Hoar, the Republica
leader of the Senate, opposing expansion, imperialism
I and slaughter, and supported by Republicans like
Governor Boutwcll, ex-Attorney General Pillsbury,
I John Sherman, Senator Mason, William E. Dodge
; and the President of the United State.-., who has de
nounced conquest as "criminal aggression," the issue
j is not partisan, and anti-imperialists do not nerd to
I resort to unsigned telegram, or covert devices. If
"committees" have been sending costly cablegrams to
be pocketed by General Otis it is far more likely that
they are composed of men who are out for annexation
by conquest and criminal aggression and war for what
there is in it for them.
REVIVE THE OLD PLAN.
T F the Board of Supervisors desires to secure a
I practical plan for adapting the present sever sys
tem of San Francisco to a new system which will
provide the city with adequate drainage, it would bet
ter abandon its attempt to get funds by transfer and
adopt the resolutions under which one of ils prede
cessors a few years ago began the same work.
As we recall the history of the former sewer com
mission, it was composed of three unsalaried com
missioners, who were authorized to employ engin
eers and supervise their work. An appropriation of
$30,000 was made and a general plan of operations
ted. But before any tangible results had been
produced the Board of Supervisors which created
this commission went out <>! ot'fice and a machine-
Democratic board came in. The members of the
latter were unable to control the patronage of the
â on as it had been embodied, and in order
to secure the appropriation for their dependents, they
repealed the sewer resolutions, dismissed the com
â  loners and turned the money into the Street De
partment fund.
This was a triumph for machine politics which re
sulted in the entire eclipse of the movement for
sewer reform. Professor Davidson, Irving M. Scott
and Engineer Mendell, as we remember, comprised
the sewer commission. They employed Engineers
Manson and Grunsky, who. with a force of draughts
men, were proceeding to reduce the sewerage prob
lem of San Francisco to a scientific basis, when the
entire scheme was interrupted by the appearance of
the political bosses and the repealing orders of the
Board of Supervisors.
Nothing has occurred since the dismissal of this
commission to render its plans obsolete, and the
work should be taken up now exactly where it was
then left off. The same gentlemen are still alive
and available for commissioners. Doubtless all three
would accept an appointment at the hands of this
Board of Supervisors and serve without compensa
tion. But at the outset the hoard must make up its
mind to one thingâ it can procure no plans that will
command respect, nor secure the services of compe
tent commissioners or engineers, unless politics is en
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 25, 1899.
tirely eliminated from the movement. If the work
is to be done by the City Hall politicians nothing
will be accomplished toward the solution of the
problem, and when the time comes to vote bonds
the people will bury the entire scheme under a shoal
of ballots.
If the present Board of Supervisors appoints a
commission it should thereafter turn it loose with
instructions to report a scientific plan, not one de
signed to promote the interests of sewer builders,
street contractors or private interests of any kind.
FLAUNTING IMMORALITY.
IN the course of its campaign for the purification of
the city from the grosser evils which corrupt its
youth The Call has accomplished much in the way
of reform. The racetrack gambling, which led to the
ruin of so many lives, has been suppressed, and the
exposure of indecent pictures in the phonograph par
lors has been restricted by the conviction and the
punishment of several of the proprietors of such
places.
In the campaign thus far the object of The Call has
been to direct attention to corrupting evils wherever
carried on, whether downtown or out at the racetrack;
but it has now to point out that some evils are the
more offensive by reason of the place where they are
maintained. A public scandal is more pernicious than
a hidden scandal, inasmuch as it poisons a larger num
ber of minds, and so a gambling room or other nefa
rious place increases in its pernicious influence in
proportion to the openness and boldness with which
it is carried on.
In the course of its fight against gambling rooms,
poolrooms and lewd picture rooms The Call has had
occasion to point out that a considerable number of
such places are conducted along Market street and
flaunt their iniquity in the face of all who pass up and
down that thoroughfare. As it is the main highway
of the retail trade of the city, the promenade along
which pass the great mass of men and women engaged
in shopping, and the avenue over which the great
htrcetcar lines run, any corrupting evil tolerated there
becomes in the very nature of things the cause of a
far greater injury to the community than if hidden
away from sight in the slums or among the less tra
versed streets, and its suppression is, therefore, of the
greater importance.
It is of course impossible to rid a community alto
gether of the vicious elements of humanity. There
will be always more or less depravity in every con
siderable city so long as the human race remains what
it is. It is not too much to demand, however, that
vice shall be made to hide itself and depravity to seek
concealment. There is no reason why dives, gam
bling rooms, nickel-in-the-slot machines, indecent
picture?, lewd and vicious places of almost all kinds
should be tolerated on Market street.
The police and the Commissioners above them have
been very indulgent to certain forms of vice along
Market street and in neighborhoods almost as well
known and frequented. There have been suspicions
iif men high in authority in police circles being inter
( sted in gambling rooms and dives and worse. Pos
sibly some of the proprietors of these places on Mar
treet may have protection in high quarters. If it
be so. it is time the public should know it. If not,
why arc they tolerated in their shameless depravity?
rROM all parts of the State come reports of
extraordinary preparations for the celebration of
Dewcy day. It is to he a festival in the true
sense of the word. The occasion is not one to be
commemorated with perfunctory ceremonies, but by
the spontaneous outbursting of popular rejoicing over
a great victory in honor of the hero who accom
plished it.
As the day is to he one of unusual interest it will
he one in which every citizen should have the privi
lege of joining. All will delight in taking some part
in the celebration of tiie day and all ought to have
full freedom to do so. The festival should be made a
true public holiday, so that all business men and their
employes may have their share in the pleasures it will
â  1 to the patriotic.
It has been the custom of a considerable number
of people in California to ignore public holidays as
far a.- possible and to keep their employes at work
and their places of business open as on ordinary days.
Such people are probably animated in adopting that
course less by motives of avarice than by an indiffer
ence to the occasion of the holiday. They lack sym
pathy with popular movements and go on about their
lions in life as if there were nothing in the world
worth living for except business.
liven such people can hardly afford to ignore
Dewey day. The great victory of last May is too
fresh in the public mind to have been forgotten by
any; and surely none is so unpatriotic as not to feel a
patriotic pride in it. Therefore, we may expert that
on this occasion every store, shop, factory and ofrke
in the city will be closed and that all will take part in
the festival. Dewey day in San Francisco should be a
holiday for every man, woman and child in the city.
except for those engaged in .trades whose daily work
is absolutely necessary to public convenience.
The English army authorities complain of a lack
of desire on the part of the males of the British Isles
to present themselves at the recruiting offices. The
cause assigned is that the condition of the labor mar
ket in the United Kingdom is so satisfactory that the
Queen's shilling a day, from which the price of rations
is deducted, is no inducement for the average English
man to don a red coat.
Advices from Samoa indicate that America, Eng
land and Germany will have to unite and whip the
South Sea islanders into good behavior. After the
savages have stopped killing each other they may
then receive the sermon of the powers on the bless
ings of peace.

After a struggle waged bitterly for
weeks past, the Mutual Electric Light
Company was granted permission yester
day to erect poles for Its wires throughout
the- city except In that portion of the
business section which is designated as
the fire limits. The action of the board
was a surprise to all parties interested.
From appearances such action was not
contemplated, and the matter passed the
board beyond recall before some members
of the majority realized what had hap
pened.
At the last meeting of the board the
order was passed to print. Subsequently
Supervisor Attridge said he had voted
under a misapprehension and it was gen
erally understood he would vote against
the measure yesterday when it came up
for final passage. It was late in the after
noon when the board was acting on un
finished business. The clerk reads merely
the title of the order, and a number of
minor matters had been finally passed
When he reached the measure which in
sures competition In lighting. This was
read, and Mayor Phelan, following his
usual custom, said: "On final passage.
Hearing no objection, finally passed."
The minority members of the board
smiled, and their exhibition of mirth at
tracted the attention of their associates.
Supervisor Phelps was the first to realize
the action that was taken, and- said: "I
object. 1 did not hear what was going
on." Messrs. Lackmann, Perrault and
Deasy objected to reopening, the matter,
saying they heard and understood and
voted with full knowledge in the premises.
Supervisor Phelps then asked to be re
corded as voting in the negative. Super
visor Kalben made the same request. Su
pervisor Attridge said he understood the
matter was not coming up at the meeting
owing to the absence of Supervisor Bying
ton at Salinas. He was informed there
was much opposition in the Mission to
streets being disfigured with additional
posts
Supervisor Lackmann cut short his ex
planation, saying the question had been
finally disposed of. He raised this as a
point of order, and Mayor Phelan held it
was well taken. Phelps thereupon ap
pealed from the decision of the chair. Six
voted against' sustaining the chairman,
â  but it required nine votes, hence the chair
was sustained.
Lackmann then said that in view of the
board placing Itself on record regarding
the matter of poles outside the fire limits,
he requested the Mayor to affix his signa
ture to the measure, making it a law be
yond recall. For reply his Honor said:
"If the clerk will hand it to me 1 will
' Sign it." This he did.
Three separate times during the subse
quent proceedings Supervisor Phelps tried
to have the minutes of the meeting show
that he voted against the measure. He
first tried to give notice tor a reconsidera
tion, but this was ruled out of order. As
it was signed and approved it had passed
out of possession of the board. Phelps
said he did not like to be "fiimfiammed out
<>!' his vote." The matter was finally
drop] â 
Dr. Perrault Introduced a resolution
that City and County Attorney Lane be
directed to dismiss the apneal in the case
of the Mutual Electric Light Company vs.
the Board of Supervisors. This was
passed. It is an appeal from the decision
of Judge Seawell, and which really fa
vored the city, as it held the Mutual Com
pany had the same right to erect poles
as that enjoyed by any other company.
This closes the incident as far as compe
tition is concerned.

a "Takedown" coming to the architect.

RUSSIANS IN ALASKA
Editor of The Call: Unjustly offend
ed and feeling the insult given to 35,000
members of the orthodox church, peo
ple and clergy, living under the pro
tection of the Stars and Stripes, I pub
licly protest against the unfounded as
sertions publicly made by Mr. Curtis
P. Coe, as published in the Examiner
on last Tuesday morning-, in the re
view on clerical club proceedings un
der he caption, "Movement to Close
Theaters on Sundays."
First Among many an American
born citizen, and as a canonically or
dained minister, I worked in this field
since ISSS. Previous to this' I was a
missionary assistant in Alaska. Among
many Americans, Governor Swineford
is one who approved of our work in
Alaska. *
Secondâ To show the falsity of Mr.
Coe's assertion, with which he so bit
terly attacks the Greek church, I am
compelled to set forth the fact that I
have been connected for some years
with the diocesan consistory, to which
all priests must report, and I do not
know of any case of the kind to which
our accuser refers. I admit there have
been instances in which our preachers
declared that non-Christian unbeliev
ers have sometimes fallen to the level
of the brute.
Third â Mr. Cue declares that in the
field, to which he has not been called,
"most of the people belonged to the
Greek church and knew nothing of
morality." I repeat that he is correct
in regard to the population, for actual
ly nine-tenths of all the people on the
Island Kodiak and the surrounding
places are members of the orthodox
church. Now, naturally arises this
question: Is this missionary of the
Baptist slighted because the parish
priests of the orthodox church in his
neighborhood prove to be a barrier to
the prosperity of his work by their zeal
in their own holy mission?
Fourth â About the "morality in the
Greek church": for want of time and
space I shall put forth but this one
statement: Look into San ntin,
which contains over a thousand pris
oners, and you will find that only five
of the number were baptized in the
orthodox church. And out of these
five there is but one Russian, one Cir
cassian, etc. Yours truly,
SEBASTIAN DABOVICH,
A priest of the Holy Orthodox Church,
San Francisco, April 19, 1899.

directions. The Bidston Hill, with a general cargo, set her course for Manila and the Lancing stood up
the coast for Puget Sound. The Bidston Hill is the first sailing vessel to leave here with a general car
go for the Government troops in the Philippines.
when the Stocktonians will be requested
