The British ship Bidston Hill sailed for
Manila yesterday and the American ship
Tacoma left Manila for San Francisco
last Sunday. The captain of the Britisher
â¢ X] i eta to make the run down in steam- i
boat time. The new tug Fearless had to
give the Bidston Hill a long tow off shore.
Side by side with the Fearless and her
tow went the tug S.->a King and the Brit
ish ship Lancing. The latter 18 bound for
md to load coal and the two vessels
made a pretty picture as they made sail
after being cast off by the tups.
The Bidston Hill did not take away
within 300 tons of her carrying capacity.
When Bhe was chartered there was no
certainty aa to when the steamers would
get back from Manila. They all came in
in a bunch, and of curse all the merchan
on hand was loaded Into them. This
left the sailing vessel with a light load,
and a considerable quantity of hay was
put aboard to fill up. Still, the vessel
d everything that the soldiers re
quire and H..ni.- supplies for the warships.
In her case it is lit. rally true that there
v as "everything from a needle to an an
chor" in her hold. There is food for man
: is:: clothing for the soldiers and
harness for the mules; needles for the
boys to mend their uniforms and under
clothing and a couple of anchors tor
Dewey'a fleet. Incidentally, the Bidston
Mill took down a large consignment ol
coffins. Even -with all this assortment she
v.i ii! away rather light.
The ship Tacoma, now on her way here
from Manila, will probably be a long time
making the voyage. She has been away
from San Francisco quite a while, and I
must be very foul. In the tropics It :
does not take the barnacles long to grow
on a vessel's bottom, and the Tacoma has
undoubtedly got her share of them. On
her arrival here she will be drydocked and
overhauled and will then start fur Manila
again with another load of cattle.
The transport. Morgan City will get
away for Manila to-day. Captain Dillon
will not go out in her and a new third
officer will also be appointed this morn
"The Ohio has begun loading at Folsom
street vharf and the Senator at Uroad-

stock about the end of the week, rhe

will probably be the only transport in

The schooner Vine, after lying off
Meiggs wharf for nearly a week, .secured
a non-union crow and got away for Nico
ki yesterday.
The missionary schooner Hiram Bing
haxn, which ' Captain Challeston of the
barkentlne Kuth thought was loajt,
reached port from Kusaie yesterday. Bat
was sixty-three days making the run,
while the Ruth came from Guam In
thirty-six days. The Hiram Hingham
was s<-'nt out by the American Hoard of
Foreign Missions about two years ago,
and since then has visited the Caroline,
Marshall, Marquesas and Gilbert groups
hi Islands, attending to the wants of the
missionaries. She comes here for an over
hauling and to be refitted. Captain Wal
kup reports that he experienced nothing
but ll^ht winds and calms during the
to San Francisco.
The Polynesian Steamship Company that
was organized in the East to run between
San Francisco, Hawaii, Tahiti and the
Marquesas has received Its quietus for
the time bring. The Cramps were Inter
ested in the line and they were to build
the ships. Plnet & Co. of this city, who
operate the line of sailing vessels now
carrying the French mail to Tahiti, were
also in the scheme and the sailing vessels
were to give way for the steamers. The
sailing vessels will continue running,
however, and no one knows when the
steamers will be built.
The Governor of Tahiti had agreed to
pay the Polynesian line a large subsidy
and that was the main incentive to the
building of the vessels. The French Gov
ernment took a hand in the business and
cabled the Governor to enter into no con
tract and give no subsidy in any circum
stance to a foreign me, as the French
Government bad under consideration the
running of a line of steamers between
France and Tahiti. That settled the
whole business and the Polynesian Steam
ship Company now exists in name only.
lieutenant Buhne took charge of the
ex-warship Thetis yesterday. The vessel
is being fixed up for a cruise to Siberia,
but it will take more 'nan a general over
hauling and a few coats of paint to make
her a comfortable vessel. One thing is
certain â ehe will never carry any rein
deer, as there is no place on deck for
them. There is a small space amidship
where a few might be corraled, but the
deer would have a lively time of it keep
ing their horns out of the way of the main
boom. The Rev. Sheldon Jackson la going
up on the vessel, but his duties and those
of Lieutenant Buhne will be confined to
making inquiries about reindeer alone, as
the vessel will not be able to carry any
back with her.
The Thetis is not considered a fit ves
sel to send to Siberia and the Navy De
partment ought to know it. She was con
demned three years ago and since then
has been lying in "rotten row" at the
navy yard. Now she goes into commis
sion as a revenue cutter and will sail
shortly for Siberia. Her hull and engines
are fairly good, but the boilers are in bad
shape and will have to be thoroughly
overhauled. The officers' quarters are
vary poor and the 3ailors do not fare
much better.

THE PRESIDIO RIOT.
Three Soldiers Convicted and Ordered
by Judge -raham to Appear for
Sentence To-Day.
The cases of C. M. Cauder, Arthur Bil
lings. P. Simmons and Charles Leiska, the
four soldiers charged with malicious mis
chief and disturbing the peace, were
called before Judge Graham yesterday
morning.
The complaining witness, Adolph Reh
feld, saloon-keeper, Greenwich and Lyon
streets, was in court with his wife and
live children, who have been made home
less by the destruction of their property
by lire caused by the soldiers during the
riot a week ago last Sunday night. Reh
feld identified. Killings, Simmons and
Leiska as three of the soldiers who threw
rocks at his place and yelled to burn it
down. He was corroborated by Henry A.
Wells, a cook, and as far as Leiska was
concerned by Policeman Herring, who
testified that he saw Leiska with his coat
off throwing rocks at the saloon and yell
ing, "Burn her down."
The Judge dismissed the case against
Cauder but found the other three defend
ants guilty and ordered them to appear
for sentence this morning. Lieutenant
Devore interceded for the men, as they
were going to Manila probably to-day to
fight for their country, and asked that they
be reprimanded and set at liberty, and the
Judge said he would give his decision to
day. __________^_
FEARS COMPETITION
FROM FILIPINOS
J. W. KERR WARNS LOCAL MAN
UFACTURES OF DANGER.
The Proposition tc Change the Tariff
Means Death to Some of the
Western Industries.
At a recent executive meeting of the di
rectors of the Manufacturers' and Pro
ducers' Association a resolution was in
troduced by J. "W. Kerr, and adopted
which was of more than ordinary signifi
cance. It relates to one of the principal
subjects that is now agitating the coun
try, whether imperialism shall or shall not
prevail. The resolution reads as follows:
Whereas, In tho dispatches from the Philip
pines to-day there appears a statement to the
effect "that the American merchants in
Manila have sent a communication tv the
Chamber of Commerce of San Francisco re
questing that body to secure certain changes
in the tariff affecting goods from that port;"
and whereas, the manufacturerers and indus
tries of the Pacific Coast and th 'He depend
ing upon them stand in as much need Of pro
tection from thf Orient to-day us ever the
Atlantic s.;il ;i!'l c!M from the manufacturers
of Europe; therefore, be it
Resolved, Thai the president of this asso
ciation communicate with the Chamber of
Commerce and keep this association informed
on this matter, to the end that no action he
taken which would injuriously affect our home
market and our struggling Industries.
In presenting the matter Mr. Kerr stat
ed that in the Philippine islands there are
millions of people who are glad to work
fur a very few rents a day, as food, cloth
ing and shelter costs but little. Already
capital is looking toward the new territory
with a view of investment, the principal
advantages being cheap fuel, cheap ma
terial, ami. above all. cheap labor. La
boring men of the United States would
not have a ghost of a show In competition
with the 10-cents-a-day natives of
Islands. While the present protective tar
iff is in force the laborers of this coast.
and the United States, in fact, have i>ut
little to fear from the islanders, but the
iiT an â¢â â¢iit the tariff is removed wages will
tumble, because neither local manufac
turers nor laborers can enter into compe
tition. He i illed attention to the first
move in this directionâ the effort (if the
merchants of -Manila to Becure certain
changes in the tariff on goods from that
port. It i? 1 the entering wedge, he said.
;.inl the association should be on its guard
to see thai it finds no crack Into which it
can be Inserted.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, April 24.â Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were idle to-day except ;
for mines and coppers. The tendency was to
dullness. The Philippine news caused realiz- j
ing sales by recent American bulls and prices
steadily sagged, closed heavy at the bottom. I
The exceptions were Norfolk and Western and |
Denver and Rio Grande preferred, which were
both bought here and by New York. Coppers
were the feature. New York Bent Anacondas
at 11%, and they closed near 12. principally
on American buying in the afternoon. Tintos !
was 46%. Spanish fours were 59. Fifty-four j
thousands pounds gold in bars was bought by
the bank. Bullion dealers expect silver to be I
twisted up soon on the smelters' combination.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, April Wheat started rather
firm, but offerings were freer than could be I
absorbed at the opening prices, and the tend
ency became downward. The weakness did
not last long, however, and a reaction set in :
that put short sellers to rout and sent prices
up with considerable energy. The rainfall was
reported insufficient In the dry belt and crop
reports were generally unfavorable, especially j
so from Michigan. The announcement of a de- ]
crease of 1,323,000 bushels in the visible, in- i
stead of only about 500.000 bushels reduction, j
as had been expected, furnished the bulls with ;
plenty of ammunition. There was also a
good export demand, thirty-eight boat loads
being disposed of at the seaboard. May
opened a. shade lower at 72%@72%c, closed with !
a gain of %STÂ»c. There was a better demand
for May than July, and the discount was
narrowed to %c, July closing at 73%@74c.
Corn was strengthened by a decrease in the
visible supply of 1,607,900 bushels and the I
probable opening of lake navigation to-morrow.
Shorts covered freely. Country offerings were !
light. May opened a shade higher, at 34@34ftc,
closed with buyers* at 34%@34%e.
Oats followed wheat and corn. Shippers !
bought heavily against cash sales. Country
longs liquidated freely, but offerings were
readily absorbed and the Improvement was
maintained. The visible decreased 24,000 bush
els. May opened a shade higher at 26%'g26%c,
closed at 24ft@24%c.
Heavy hog receipts and lower prices at the
yards weakened provisions. Packers sold
freely. Shipments of meats were fair at 2,989,- !
000 pounds. May pork opened 7%0 lower at ',
$9 05, closed at $9.
The range in lard and ribs was narrow.

DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
Receipts were 148 boxes Cherries and 302
chests Strawberries.
Crop reports are now coming in freely and
the indications remain the same as a fortnight
ago, viz.: Peaches heavy, Pears moderate.
Apricots light, Cherries very light and Prunes
considerably less than a full crop, some dis
tricts reporting almost a failure, while others
make a better showing. These conditions may
be modified, one way or the other, later on,
however.
Berries were lower. Cherries kept up under
slender receipts. Limes advanced. Lemons
and Oranges were unchanged. .

THE STOCK MARKET.
Local securities were very active on the
morning session and prices showed numerous
fluctuations, Paauhau Plantation advanced to
$43 r>o, Giant Powder t ntra Water
to $71 12'->, Hawaiian Commercial to $105 and
Hutchinson to $34 25. The general tone of the
market was strong.
Jn the afternoon Hawaiian advanced an
other point, but others of the leaders showed
some weakness.
Mining stocks were stronger at an ad
vance- of a few cents. The telegram from the
pump said: "Elevator working Steadily.
Water In the 1750 level winze is 31 feet 4
inches below the station. The second eleva
tor arrived last night."
The following ore shipments were made by
rail from the mines of Eureka district, Ne
vada, for the week ending April 21: Diamond
mine, GG.340 pounds; Eureka Consolidated, 155,
--730; Jackson, 67.460; Joe Flynn-Mineral Hill,
36,000.
Annual meetings are announced as follows:
North Star Mining Company, May 10; Origi
nal Empire Mill and Mining Company, May
10; Union Gold Mining Company, May 6; Grass
Valley Water Company, May 10; East Sierra
Nevada Mining Company, May S.
At the Consolidated California and Virginia
mine during the past week the C. and C.
shaft has been cleaned out and all necessary
repairs made for a depth of 36 feet below the
1850 level, the shaft being ready to receive the
new elevator. On the surface work has been
continued on the hoisting engine for the 1750
winze and the compressor to be used for work
below that point. 1400 levelâ The west drift
has been extended 17 feet; total distance west
from C. and C shaft, 470 feet The face is in
soft hanging wall rock, showing less water
than for the past two weeks. 16.10 levelâ The
bulkhead of brick and cement to retain the
water from the upper levels has been com
pleted. The raise from this level to the drift
connecting with the Opnir shaft has been
cleaned out :.i"l the connecting drift is being
repaired, as this drift and raise must be kept
open for ventilation. The pipe system from
the 1650 down through the old stopes connect
ing with the northwest passage on the 1750
Is being repaired, so that the water may be
carried into the old stopes through to the
Houth end of the stopes. 1750 level â The winze
station is being repaired and put in order to
receive the compressed air hoist. The water
in the winze is now 29 feet below the sta
tion.
In the Best & Belcher and Gould & Curry
mines, in the Oshlston shaft connection, a
light platform has been built on the Sutro
tunnel level to catch up water falling down
the shaft, and a fair percentage of the water
is caught up and sent out to the Sutro tun
nel. The water has fallen in the shaft during
the past week 3 feet 10 inches. Total depth
below station. 70 feet 4 inches. They have
removed the boiler from the Bonner shaft to
this shaft and have started in the collar of
the shaft getting timbers in place preparatory
to repairing the shaft.
In the Sierra Nevada mine, on the 140 level,
the raise started in the north drift at a point
223 feet north from the west drift to connect
with the Riley tunnel hay been advanced 21
feet: total height, 79 feet; top in porphyry and
clay, with seams of Quartz through it. 900 level
âThe north drift started in west crosscut 2
has been advanced 12 feet; total length. 20
feet; face in a mixture of porphyry and clay.
In the Ophir mine, in the old Central tunnel
workings, the second crosscut from the north
west drift was extended 5 feet; total, 30 feet.
In the last five feet the formation has
changed. After passing through solid quartz
a siram of black clay was found, beyond the
clay a mixture of clay, quartz and porphyry,
and now in the face more quartz is found.
In the west drift an air passage has been
opened, giving much better air. This drift
was cleaned out for 13 feet; total, 139 feet,
through very heavy ground that required care
ful timbering. They have a few seta now to
put in before the ground will be all caught
up. The Sutro tunnel connection was ex
tended 12 feet; total, DO feet; face in hard
hanging wall rock. The drift northeast from
the Sutro tunnel, following a stringer of
quartz, has been extended 4 feet; total length,
12 feet The quartz Btill continues through the
formation and is irregular. The walls of this
stringer are now mixed with quartz. There is
a well-defined clay seam on the footwall of the
quartz stringer.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
Branch Hydrographic Office, U. S. X., Mer
chants" Exchange, San Francisco, Cal.,
April 24, ISTO.
Information has bern received from Colonel
O. F. Long, United States Army, general super
intendent of the army transport service, that
the Government transports Grunt. Sheridan,
Sherman, Hancock and Warren, plying between
this port and Manila, have all been supplied
With a complete set of Coston signals. The
distinguishing signal, which la displayed at
night, is a red. white and blue in succession.
The hospital ship Relief is als-i supplied with
the red. white and blue transport signal, and
in addition she displays a red light followed by
a green star, which is the hospital service dis
tinguishing signah
The funnel mark of the service is red, white
and blue bands.
The chartered transports of the Government
have not yet been supplied with signals, but if
they are in future notification will be given.
C. G. CALKINS.
Lieutenant. I. S. N . in charge.

NOTE-In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time The
second time column Rives the "~ on * ,i de 5*
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The neignis
Kiven are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

INSTEAD OF THE
SURGEON'S KNIFE
The Tragic Suicide of
H. Garcelon.
ILLNESS PARTLY FANCIED
BLEW OUT HIS BRAINS WITH A
REVOLVER.
His Physicians Tried to Persuade
Him That His Sickness Was Not
as Serious as He
* Imagined.
Alameda Office San Francisco Call, \
1428 Park street, April 24.
Because he fancied that he would have
to undergo a surgical ration and
dreaded the ordeal. Horace Garcelon,
formerly a bookkeeper in the employ of
Lev! Strauss & Co., blew out his brains
this afternoon with a 44-caliber Colt's re
volver of ancient pattern.
Garcelon had been living with his wid
owed mother at 1553 Rail] avenue. He |
had long complained of kidney trouble,
and about three months ago pave up his
position on account of his ailment. Drs. |
Stafford and Keys, ho had been attend- ;
ing him, assured him that his sickness j
was not serious, and he insisted that he j
would die unless an operation was per- I
formed, which the physicians declared i
was unnecessary.
Garcelon's mother left him in the house I
about 3 i clock this afternoon while she I
went to visit a neighbor. She returned \
nt 4 o'clock to lind the house filled with
Graoke. She detected the smell of burned j
powder and at once imagined the worst. ,
Hastening to Joseph Murizio, a neighbor, i
she told him of her fears. Marizio rushed !
to the house and found Garcelon lying on !
his bed with the clothing saturated with
hia blood. There was a gaping wound in j
the head, which told plainly of the cause
of death. The deceased left no message
explaining why he had taken his life.
Three chambers of the revolver were
empty, but only one of the shots fired
took "effect. One went through the side
of the building and another cut a wire
holding a picture, dropping It on the
bod beside the suicide.
Deceased was about 45 years old and a
widower. Deputy Coroner Fowler de
cided not to remove the body to the !
Morgue, but will hold the inquest at the
house.
The doctors who had been attending
Garcelon state that his sickness was not
as serious as he imagined and i eve that
he worried over the matter until he be
came temporarily insane. As an evidence
that Garcelon had contemplated suicide
for some time and that he had finally
iixed upon to-day as the time for his
self-destruction, it is stated that '<..â  has
been urging his mother and daughter to
take- a trip into the country for several
weeks past.
This morning he visited Rev. John Han
non of the Methodist Church, South, and
asked to be baptized. The minister want
ed him to wait until next Sunday night,
ns it was not customary to attend to
such matters on week days. < rarcelon
insisted that he wished to join the church
at once, so that if anything happened to
him he would be prepared to meet his
Maker at once. His earnestness so im
pressed Rev. Mr. Hannon that he finally
administered the rite of baptism.

hlgh. Among the honors he has held

feel that Masters has used his pen a little more freely than he
â¢ hence their summary action.
The academic council met this morning and the matter came up there.
â¢' the professors introduced the fiubject, stating that he thought the
the hook such as to warrant the most stringent measures being taken
; â¢:â  ose responsible for Its publication. The council with but little dis
â¢\ unanimously ordered the following notice to be placed upon the bul
â -1 r 1 1 :
"At a meeting of the faculty of the college of letters, held this flay, it
was Voted, In accordance with a recommendation of the academic council,
that the â  hief editor of this year's Blue and Gold be dismissed from the uni
versity.
"It was ordered, further, that notice of this action be posted on the unl
verslty bulletin board.
"Atl "JAMBS ST'TTON,
"Recorder of the Faculties."
When seen this afternoon the members of the faculty declined to discuss
the question or to state the special parts of the book which they considered
deserving of censure. They admitted that not one of the instructors who
had been most heavily Joshed had s< nt In any complaint, but insisted that
their attitude was toward the book as a whole.
President Martin J. Kejfogg said: "This is a most unfortunate affair. In
common with the other members "f the faculty, though, I do not see what
else could have be< n Blue and Gold has this time passed beyond all
bearable limits, and l^tead of the legitimate fun which all can enjoy, has
Stooped in vulgarit- ....r which ! feel very much ashamed. Its tone is some
thing which ap a ' wverslty we cannot possibly tolerate. It would seriously
affect "ijr dignity to let such a mntter pass by unnoticed. I do not care to
State what portions of the bonk we think the most objectionable; It is enough
that from cover to cover the faculty feel it has passed beyond the point at
which we can allow such license to go."
The opinion of thf president was echoed by most of the faculty. Professor
Walter Magee, who comes in for several caricatures, explained that it was the
general tone of the publication which, he thought, deserved censure, "I don't
>ie said, "how any student can dare to publish such things as appear in
this year's annual without expecting to be dismissed from college. Our atti
tude is the only one which can be consistently taken. Of course, we regret
very fhueh to inflict this penalty, especially upon a student who otherwise is
held in esteem. But I fail to see what else can be done. The dignity of the
college, and its reputation without, cannot surfer such indiscriminate lam
pooning to be ignored. It was ungentlemanly, to say the least, for any college
man to give such an impression of our college life to the public as must be
gathered from the pages of the Blue and Cold. It represents us as anything
but gentlemen, and that Is enough to warrant our dismissal of the editor."
Masters was called to the president's office this afternoon and President
Kellogg there acquainted him with the faculty's decision. He was told simply
that he would be allowed to remain at the university no longer and that he
might consider himself dismissed. No word of explanation was given save
that as editor of the Hlue and Gold he was deserving of the extreme penalty
Which the faculty could impose, and that he must submit to their decision.
When the news of this became generally known throughout the university
it created a tremendous excitement. The student body as a whole resents the
action of the professors. Petitions at once began to circulate asking that the
decision of the academic council be reconsidered. Practically every student In
the college will attach his signature, and every effort will be made to have
Masters reinstated. Several members of the faculty, though, state that their
action will stand, and that on no account will their order be revoked. The
college men are talking of appealing to the Board of Regents-, and rash
threats are being made that the whole junior class will leave the university
unless Masters is taken back.

FABIOLA DAY
CONDEMNED BY
THE MINISTERS
Dr. Dille Leads the
Opposition.
GENEROUS OFFER IS MADE
TWO LADIES WOULD PREFER
TO GIVE THOUSANDS.
Lady Managers Are Surprised That
the Matter Has Been Brought Up
and Are Busy Selling
Tickets.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, April 24.
Many of the Oakland ministers attended
the usual Monday meeting In San Fran
cisco to-day and decided to denounce in
every way possible the holding 1 of a Fabi
ola Derby day. Rev. E. R. Dille. D. D.,
presided and made a short speech, stat
ing that the curse of Alameda County
and at this time of San Francisco is
Emeryville. He referred to the pernicious
influences of the racetrack and said that
such an institution as Fahiola should be
far above accepting any of the proceeds
from such a disreputable concern as the
racetrack.
Dr. Dille said that many ladies in this
city condemned the project and an
nounced that Mrs. Cooke, who has re
cently given a very valuable children's
annex to the hospital, and another lady
whose name he was not at liberty to men
ticn had assured him that they would
give $1000 apiece rather than have a bene
fit for Fabiola at the racetrack.
Several other ministers expressed the
same sentiments, and It was decided to do
everything possible to let it be known
that the ministers objected seriously to
the Fabiola Derby day. The Alameda
County ministers present were: Rev. C.
R. Brown, Rev. Dr. E. R. Dille, Rev. E.
S. Chapman, Rev. A. T. Needham, Rev.
W. W. Si udder. Rev. B. J. Perry, Rev.
T. H. Robinson and Rev. L. J. Garber.
Next Wednesday evening at their reg
ular prayer-meeting these ministers will
cause their sentiments to be known.
The lady managers of Fabiola are doing
everything possible to make the Derby
day a success. Tickets are being sold ail
over the county, and a large attendance is
assured. One of the lady managers who
told of the action of the ministers
said: "I thought this matter was thor
oughly threshed out last year. I do not
think there Is anything new to be said.
If I remember rightly. Dr. Dille led the
opposition i" us last year, and somebody
prayed that it might rain on that occa
sion. It did rain, but whether in answer
to prayer or not 1 do not know. But I do
know that we received a substantial bene
fit tor Fnbiola. Rabbi Levy took our
part, and 1 presume that public opinion
is divided the same to-day as it was last
year. All the ladies on the board, I must
admit, are not unanimous on this propo
sition; but the majority of us arc. and
personally 1 cannot see any difference \x -
tween a gambler giving a check to Fabi
ola or putting one on a church plate.
I hope it will be a glorious day and that
there will be a large attendance, for Fa
biola needs .nil the encouragement that
can be given it."
Passing Counterfeit Coin.
Joseph Ray and Thomas Phillips, wh>>
Rive their occupations a* waiters, wre
arrested yesterday morning by Policemen
McGee and Coleman on Turk street for
having counterfeit money in their posses
sion. The officers found that the two
men had been passing counterfeit half
dollars on saloon keepers in the "tender
loin district", and arrested them on Turk
street. When searched at the City Prison
Ray had three counterfeit half dollars in
his pockets and. Phillips two. Secret Ser
vice Agent Brown was notified and ques
tioned the men, as several plaster of
parls casts of half dollars were found in
tht^r rooms by the two officers, but he
could not get anything out of them.

AN ENTERTAINING SOCIAL.
The Pacific Coast Women's Press As
sociation Discusses "Incidents
of Travel."
There is always some distinctive feature
about the social meetings of the Pacific
Coast Women's Press Association that
renders these gatherings peculiarly in
teresting and instructive. At the gather-
Ing of the association yesterday afternoon
"Incidents of Travel" was the topic, and
most refreshingly was it handled. In
stead of a dry discussion, each of the
speakers told of some experience or ob
servation, thereby lending a personality
to their remarks which made them all the
more interesting. Those who spoke on
travel were: -Mrs. M. F. Grey, Miss Bessie
Riimsey, Mrs. Alice Klngsbury Cooley,
Mrs. W. A. Truesdell. Miss S. Hartman
and Mrs. E. M. North. Mrs. Abbie E.
Krebs, the president of the association,
presided at the meeting and introduced
the speakers.
In addition to the literary part of the
programme there was a piano solo by
Miss Katheiine Nolan and a vocal solo
by Miss Cornelia Stanley. Miss Rita Mar
chand recited a humorous poem by Miss
H. X. Skidmore. Among those who were
present were Mrs. Mary E. Hart, the
first vie? president of the association,
who has just returned from a six weeks'
visit to L.HB Angeles, and Mrs. E. F. Wel
ler of the same place, who is a visiting
member of the association.

TWO VERDICTS.
Juror Patrick Kelly Files a Special
Opinion in the Inquest on
Christine Buttner.
The rare spectacle of a Coroner's Jury
rendering two verdicts In the c me case
at the same time was witnessed yesterday
at the inquest on .ne body of Mrs. Chris
tine Buttner, the demented widow who
cut her throat with a carving knife last
Baturday at her residence, 20U Taylor
street.
Clara Buttner, stepdaughter of the de
ceased, after telling the jury what she
had seen of the horrible act, said that
Mrs Buttner had brooded for a long time
over the delusion that Mr. and Mrs.
Kroeger held her responsible for the
death of their child. The child had be< n
placed in thr- care of Mrs. Buttner, and
died of cholera infantum. She had done
for the ehilil all that was In her power,
and the Kroegera had not even thought
of blaming her. Bul Bhe was under the
halucination that the Kroegers were ac
cusing her and talking about her. She
was continually afraid of being arrested.
Ml the jurors but one Bigned" a verdict
that the deceased bad committed Buicide
while temporarily Insane. Patrick Kelly,
a hatmaker of 51 Fifth street, returned a
verdict of suicide, with the recommenda
tion "that the Coroner censure Mrs. "Wolff
and Mr. Kroeger for the manner in which
they had persecuted the deceased by us-
Ing scandalous language about her/
Coroner Hill approved and signed the
maiority verdict, but declined to approve
of !\tr. Kelly's recommendation.

RAILROAD EXTENSION.
Seven Hundred Men at Work on the
Santa Barbara Line.
Colonel George Stone of the firm of Mc-
Murtry & Stone, contractors for grading
the Santa Barbara extension of the
Southern Pacific, arrived in the city Sun
day night from the scene of the work.
The distance to be graded is fifty-one
miles. Owing to the ruggedness of the
country along the base of the Santa Ynex
mountains, the heavy work cannot be
finished short of eighteen months. Big
bridges and culverts must be put in, as
the river beds carry torrents of water in
the rainy w ason.
The distance from San Francisco to Los
Angeles, via San Tails Obispo and Santa
Barbara, is not less than that of the
route through the San Joaquin Valley and
across the Tehachapi from this city to
Los Angeles, but the grade of the lint
along the coast is nominal and trains
may be moved at a high rate of speed.
The contractors have now 700 men at
work between Surf and Elwood and the
force will be increased.
FUNERAL OF JUDGE BREEN.
Services Held at St. Agnes Church
and the Remains Taken to
San Juan.
Funeral services wore held over the re

yesterday, Rev. Father William Kirby
officiating. The pall-bearers were Judge
William P. Lawlor, William D. English,
P. J. Herein P. P. Deering. Thomas B.
Flynn ana Garret McEnerney. The re
mains were taken to the old home of
Judge Breen in San Juan, where final in
terment will take place to-day.

ANTHRACITE COAL.
Judge Morrow Decides That It Must
Pay Duty if It Does Not Contain
Sufficient Fixed Carbon.
United States Judge Morrow yesterday
handed down an opinion in the matter of
the appeal of Charles P. Coles from a
ruling of the Collector of the Port and
from a ruling of the Board of General Ap
praisers of New York.
On July 24, 1897, Coles imported from
Swansea." Wales, a cargo of coal on the
British ship Muskoko. The Collector
classified it as containing less than 92 per
cent of fixed carbon and held that it was
therefore dutiable under the act of July
24. 1897, at the rate of 67 cents per ton.
The Board of General Appraisers upheld
the Collector. Judge Morrow decided
that coal of any description or name con
taining less than 92 per cent of fixed
in is liable to duty, while anthracite
coal containing 02 per cent or more fixed
carbon is duty free. He therefore upheld
the Collector and the Board of Apprais
ers.

SEWER SYSTEM
PROPOSITION IS
UNDER THE BAN
Question of Funds
May Defeat It.
ENGINEERS WANT THEIR PAY
POWER OF SUPERVISORS IS
QUESTIONED.
Transfer of Money While Creditors
Remain Unpaid May Precipitate
a Long and Acrimonious
Legal Controversy.
The Mayor and the Hoard of Supervis
ors have declared in favor of a bond Issue
to provide funds for the establishment of
an Improved sewer By stem, but there is a
strong probability that the question will
not be submitted to the people this year
on account of lack of funds to insure the
performance of tha necessary preliminary
work.
The Board of Supervisors yesterday I
passed to print an ordinance declaring the j
Intention of the board to appropriate the
sum of $20,000 for the purpose of fray
ing the expense of preparing the neces-
Bar} plane and estimates for the proposed
sewer system prior to the submission of
the question to the people. It is slated In
this ordinance, or resolution, that the
money cannot be appropriated for this
purpose until the end of the present fiscal
ynar. at which time a surplus in the
street lighting fund can be transferred
to the street fund and thus become avail
able, It Is on the question of the ability
of the board to transfer this money from
one fund to another as Intended thai the
whole question hinges.
Marsden Man eon and C. E. Grunsky,
who have been named as engineers to
conduct the work of perfecting the plans
for a sewer system and who are to be
paid $4000 each for their services, called
upon Auditor Wells yesterday and asked
his advice as to what course they should
pursue. They declared their unwilling- j
ness to proceed with i he work unless they I
were assured of compensation and they
wanted to know what prospect there was j
of setting the money that the Board of j
Supervisors promised to appropriate at
the end of the present fiscal year.
Auditor Wells informed them that there
was a large deficiency in the Street De- I
partment fund and that there are now in j
his hands many demands against that j
fund, some of them dating back as far as j
January of the present year, which re
main unpaid for lack of funds. The con
tractors who hold these demands have
been patiently waiting- for their money
with the understanding that at the end
of the fiscal year the surplus in the street
lighting fund would be transferred to the
Street Department fund and their de
mands against the latter fund would then
be audited and paid. The Auditor said
that if the Board of Supervisors entered
into new contracts for the use of this j
money with the knowledge that debts still
unpaid were left unprovided for the con- i
tractors would probably claim such act
on the part of the board to be Illegal and
would take such legal action as would
effectually tie up all of the funds Involved
In the controversy. So far as the engi
neers are concerned, the question is left
in so unsatisfactory a condition that it is j
likely they will refuse to go on with the [
work until they are given more substan- j
tial assurance that they will be paid for i
the labors they are expected to perform.
Following is the ordinance passed to print
by the Board of Supervisors yesterday:
Whereas, under section 4 of the muni
cipal Improvement act, approved March 19,
l^'.i, this board. contemplating permanent â 
public Improvements, the cost of which
Will be too great to be paid 'it of the
onlinnry annual income and revenue of the
municipality, is required to have plans and
estimates of the rust of such improvements
made prior to submitting the question of
issuing bonds at a special election to the j
qualified voters of this city and county; and
whereas, thin board hereby determine*
that the public Interests demand the eon- j
struction of main OUtlet sewers to promote
and improve the sanitary condition of the
city and county and insure drainage and
protection to property, and will provide at
the proper time for a special election, to
be held as soon after the plans and esti
mates of. cost are received as the provis
ions of the statute will permit; and
whereas, this board, in order to afford
the relief demanded by our citizens, is re
quired to take all preparatory steps to as
certain and determine the costs to be in
curred from an examination of the condi
tion as to defective sewerage of the pres
ent system, and in the additional construc
tion of main sewers In the various districts
now Buffering from a want of sewerage
fodlif and whereas, no appropriation
has been made this fiscal year for this pur
pose, but the condition of the street light
fund from the expenditures heretofore
made, and the limit of the amounts to be
p;ild monthly for the services of light
ing the public streets show that there will
be a surplus exceeding $40,000 unexpended
in said fund at the close of the fiscal
year; therefore,
Resolved, That this board hereby appoints
C S. Tllton. C. E. Grunsky and Marsden
Manson. civil engineers, to constitute a
board of engineers, whose duty it shall be
to prepare and submit plans and estimates
of the expense to be Incurred, and to that
end it Is necessary to oorrect the defects
and inefficiencies of existing sewers and to
prescribe the salient features of the sys
tem required for districts not now sew
ered; to determine the method of sewage
collection and disposal and the points of
outfall; to determine the location, size and
character of the main sewers for each of
the districts Into which the city Ib divided
by topographical features, and the mode
of delivering district sewage to the points
of the outfall; to recommend the order in
which- main sewers and works should be
constructed and to designate those which
should be constructed at once, with a
plan of the same; to make an estimate of
the cost Of constructing the main sewers
and works suggested for immediate con
struction, which plans and estimates must
be submitted and are hereby required to be
filed with this board on or before October
16, ISM.
Resolved, That the compensation to be
paid each of paid engineers is hereby fixed
at $4000. and the expenses necessitated by
such employment In the examination, sur
veys, compilation of data, preparation of
plans and estimates of expense and other
incidental expenses required by said board
of engineers Is hereby fixed at $8000, the
whole or so much of said appropriation to
be expended on said engineers as may be
necessary in their Judgment to obtain all
the Information required.
ItPFolved. That the board pledges Its In
tention to, and will. In the month of June,
after all the liabllit'Â«.Â» on the street light
fund have been met and provided for, trans
fer from the street light fund to an appro
propriation herein created, to be known as
and for the services of the board Of en
gineers herein appointed, the mm of $20,000,
to enable this work to be prosecuted and
completed as provided for, in defraying the
' expenses of the employment and Incidental
expenses of said board of engineers.
Resolved, That the Auditors Â»nd Treas
urer's attention is respectfully called to the
provisions of this resolution, and said of
ficers are further empowered and requested
to consider this appropriation as being
made bo that the demands for the Inci
dental expenses to be incurred by the said
Board of Engineers shall be audited and
paid after the same have been allowed by
this board and until such time In the
month of June when the transfer can be
legally made: and â ]
Resolved. That each of the engineers ap- i
pointed shall be entitled to and shall re
recelve part payment of the compensation
herein provided, an follows: On July 3,
the sum of $1M)O; on August 21, $10u0; on
October 16, $1500.
Re-solved, That this board hereby desig
nates the room formerly occupied by the
architects of the City Hall for use of the
Board of Engineers If necessary.
Resolved. That this board will hereafter I
provide for the appointment of not to ex
ceed two eminent engineers as consulting
engineers to advise and confer with the en-
Klneers herein above named, and to examine
their work as It progresses and plans sub
mitted to report thereon in order that the
plans and estimates may be corrected If
required and approved before further action
la had or taken.

NOTICE is hereby given by order of the Board
of Directors of the OCEANIC STEAMSHIP
COMPANY that a meeting of the stockholders
of. aid Company has been called by said
Board, to be held on FRIDAY, the second day
of June, A. D. It!*'', at eleven o'clock In the
forenoon of said day, at the principal place of
business of said Company, nt the building
where the said Board of Directors usually
meets, namely, at the office of said Company,
number 327 Market St., In the City and County
of San Francisco, State of California; that
the cbject of said meeting is to consider and
act upon the proposition that said Company
create a bonded Indebtedness of two million j
five hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000 00); in j
United States gold coin, for the purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
of its steamships and their equipment, for
use In the business of this corporation, and
to purchase and pay for any other property
within the purposes of this Company: and to
secure the bonded Indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon its steam
and sailing ships and all other property of
said Company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.
By order of the Board of Directors of the
Oceanic Steamship Company.
[Corporate Seal.] E. H. SHELDON.
Secretary of the Oceanic Steamship Com-

NOTICE. â¢
To All Whom It May Concernâ Notice Is
hereby given that on the 17th day of April.
1880. the Bakersfield and I^js Angeles Bail-
way Company filed its application In writing,
in the office of the Clerk of the Superior
Court of the City and County of Kan Fran-
cisco, addressed to the Superior Court of said
county, making application for the voluntary
dissolution of the Bukersfield and Los An-
geles Railway Company, which said petition
and application stated that at a meeting of
the stockholders of said company on the 13th
day of April. 1899, called for the purpose of
passing upon the question, it was resolved to
nave a voluntary dissolution of the corpora-
tion, and that such resolution was passed by I
a two-thirds vote of all the stockholders, and I
that all the claims and demands against the
corporation, of every kind, nature and descrip-
tion, have been fully paid and discharged.
Wherefore. It was ordered by the said Su- I
perior Court upon the filing of said petition I
and application that the undersigned as i
Clerk of said Court, give a notice of such ap- |
plication by publication In some newspaper |
published in the City and County of San j
Francisco, for a period of thirty-five days.
Therefore, all persons having any Interest in
the matter of said application or desiring to 1
oppose the granting of the same are here- !
by notified to fIU" in my office, on or before i
the 22d day of May, 1899, any and all objec- I
tions which they may have to the granting of j
""in testimony whereof, I have hereunto set my !
hand and the official seal of said court this
the 17th day of April. 1899.
(Seal WILLIAM A. DEANB,
County Clerk and ex-Offlcio Clerk of the
Superior Court^ r M THOMpgON
Deputy County Clerk and ex-Offlcio Deputy j
Clerk of the Superior Court. .

To Print, Publish and Distribute the De-
linquent Tax List for 1898.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE

County of San Francisco, April 25, 1899.

(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervis-
ors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived in open session of the Board of Su-
pervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON

printing, publishing and distributing of
the delinquent tax list for the year 1898,
to contain the names of the persons and
a description of the property delinquent
and the amount of taxes and costs due
opposite each name, and a notice unless
the taxes delinquent, together with the

PROPOSALS
For Burial of Indigent Dead.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE
Board of Supervisors of the City and !
County of Sun Francisco, April 25."1599.
In accordance with Resolution No. 262S
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Super-
visors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived in open session of the Board Of
Supervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON,
May 8, 1599, from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for
the interment of such of the indigent
dead as are required to be buried at the
charge of the City and County of San
Francisco, including the City and County j
Smallpox Hospital, for the term of one
year, beginning July 1, 1899, and ending
June 30, 1900, in such cemetery or ceme-
teries in San Mateo County wherein
burials may be allowed.
NOTICE.â The coffins to be made of
three-quarter-inch surface redwood clear
lumber, to be lined with muslin and pil-
lowed inside, and to be shellacked and
varnished outside, and the tops to be
fastened down with not less than six
screws. In all cases where persons have
died from smallpox or other contagious
diseases the coffin shall be lined with No.
10. zinc instead of with muslin, and her-
metically sealed, and all coffins used
must be subject to the inspection and ap-
proval of the officer In charge of the in-
stitution from which the body of the
decedent is to be burled, and must in all
cases be taken to such institution for the
purpose of having the deceased placed
therein.
No bid will be considered from any par-
ty who has not been engaged in the un-
dertaking business in this City and Coun-
ty and conducted said business in a rep-
utable manner for a period of at least six
months immediately preceding the date
hereof.
The grave to be excavated to a depth
of at least six feet.
No body to be removed from the insti-
tution where it lies awaiting burial with-
in a period of twenty-four hours after-
death, except by express order of the
Superintendent or officer in charge of
said institution.
The contractor will be required in all
; cases on the death of the indigent person
from infectious disease to inter the body
* of said decedent within such time as may
I be designated by notice fmm the Superin-
i tendent or officer in charge of the institu-
! tl<n where the death may have occurred,
! and in all other cases within thirty-six
| hours after notification by the proper of-
ficer.
The contractor will further be required
I to convey the bodies of decedents com-
: mitted to his charge for interment direct
from the institution from which he re-
ceives the same to the cemetery, and then
I and there bury the same. And will not
be permitted upon any consideration to
convey such bodies to any intermediate
â  resting-place, or to keep the same in his
i custody for a longer period than that re-
I quired for their transit to the cemetery.
Should the friends of any decedent ap-
ply at the institution where such deced-
ent may be lying awaiting burial, for per-
mission to bury the same, and prove to
the Superintendent or officer in charge of
said institution that their purpose is to
provide a decent burial, and that they
have no intention and will not allow the
body to be used for the purpose of dissec-
tion, then and in that case the body of
said deceased shall be delivered to said
: friends, who shall take charge thereof
i and make the necessary arrangements for
I the funeral with any undertaker they
! may think fit; but in such event the con-
tractor aforesaid shall have no claim
against the city for the burial of said de-
cedent.
The party to whom the contract may
be awarded shall be required, in all cases,
to procure from the Superintendent of the
cemetery wherein the burials have been j
made a certificate of the proper burial of ;
each person under the provisions of his i
contract and of these specifications; and
such certificate must, in all cases, be at-
tached to the demand of said contractor
when presented to the Board of Super-
visors for approval, and must state the
names of the decedents and the institu-
tions from which the body was procured.
In case of the violation of any of the
above conditions, the contract shall be
terminated by said City and County.
The party to whom this contract is
awarded will be required, prior to or at
the time of the execution of said contract,
to pay the cost of advertising this notice
In three daily newspapers, and to also
pay the cost of the publication in the of-
ficial newspaper of the Resolution award-
ing him the contract.
All interments of indigent persons the
expenses of which must be borne by the
City and County (other than those from
I any of the public institutions) must be
authorized by the Health Officer issuing
a permit therefor, and all bills for such
interments to be approved by the Health
, Officer prior to being presented to this
Board. All demands ror the burial of in-
digent persons from any of the public in-
stitutions to be approved by the Superin-
tendent of thtÂ» institution at which the
death occurs, prior to being presented to
this Board.
Bidders will estimate and state a speci-
fic amount for each interment.
In order to preserve the uniformity and
to facilitate the award, tho Board has
resolved to receive no bids unless made
upon blank forms prepared by the com-
mittee, and a certified check on some
bank in the City and County of San Fran-
cisco for the sum of five hundred ($500)
dollars, in favor of and made payable to
the Clerk of the Hoard of Supervisors,
must be deposited by the bidder, with his
bid, conditioned that if the proposal is
accepted and the contract awarded, and
if the bidder shall fail or neglect to pay
the printing charges, exrrute the con-
tract and give a bond of five thousand
($5000) dollars, required within six days af-
ter the award is made. then, and iti that
case, the said sum shall be collected and
paid into the City and County Treasury
by said Clerk as liquidated damages for
t-uch failure and neglect.

all bids if tho public pood so require.

Against Vice Has
Again Brought
Good Re
sults.
ONCE more has public opinion
forced act/on on the part of
the Police Department, and
THE CALL'S crusade against
flaunting vice has again borne
fruit.
Three notorious gambling
houses â the Cafe Royal, at Fourth
and Market streets; Metropole,
823 Market street, and Palace sa
loon, 869 Market street â were
raided last evening, and a number
of frequenters of these debasers
of morals were taken to prison.
Months ago THE CALL began
its crusade, showing the perni
cious evils which were permitted
by a complaisant Police Depart
ment to debauch and corrupt the
youth and young manhood of San
Francisco. The vile dens of China
town, where illicit games were in
progress night and day, nere ex
posed, and the slave traffic of that
portion of the community shown in
such colors as to attract the at
tention of the entire city, and the
result was an upheaval in the per
sonnel of the Chinatown squad
and the constant raiding of the
dens.
Months ago attention was called
to the faro games running at
prominent hotels, and again public
opinion compelled action on the
part of the head of the Police De
partment.
The evils of racetrack gam
bling, with the terrible sequels of
crime in the business community,
resulting in defaulters, absconders,
fugitives from justice, murders
and suicides, were fully set forth

Harry Wilson, the mulatto In the City

MISS CARROLL WAS
NOT INCOMPETENT
CHARGES AGAINST HER DIS
MISSED BY THE BOARD.
The Evidence Given Did Not Prove !
She Lacked Either Tact
or Scholarship.
The Board of Education mot last even
ing for the purpose of trying Miss Lydla
Carroll on charges of incompetence, lack
of tact and scholarship.
There was a full board present when
the meeting was called and the members ;
got down tii business at once by demand
ing from the Superintendent the list of i
those who were booked for trial. There j
were threeâ Kilpatrick, who prevented
the progress of his trial a week ago by
writs of prohibition from the Superior
Court; Dr. Wiedeman, who was too ill
to appear, and Miss Carroll. Miss Carroll
was ready and asked that her case bo
brought before the board In executive ses
sion. Her wishes were acceded to. and
the boardroom was cleared of every one
but the defendant and her attorney, F.
P. Deering, and the board went into com
mittee of the whole.
There were five witnesses examined,
and their examination occupied from !â¢ '
o'clock until 12. Then the committee of
the whole adjourned and reported that I
the charges had not been sustained by the
evidence. The report was adopted by the
board without dissent.
The charges were brought by Joseph
O'Connor, principal of the Mission High
School, of which the accused teacher is
vice principal.
When the board adjourned it was until
next Monday evening, when the charges
against the other teachers mentioned will
be brought up and perhaps tried. The
trial of Kilpatrick is expected to be a
long one.

I Vantage point to another until the
people began to feel that decency
and morality would finally rule.
The Examiner then joined the
crusade and gave able and welcome
assistance to THE CALL in its
cleansing efforts. Following close
ly on the lines laid down by THE
CALL, the Examiner did valiant
work in the interests of decency,
and last night's raids were the
beginning of the end of the reign
I of evil in San Francisco.
Acting under instructions from Chief
Lees, Sergeant Conboy, Officers Tyrrell
and Esola and posse began their evening's
work. They were all in citizen's dress,
and although recognized by many hang
ers-on about the places afterward raided,
the alarm was not given, as it was
thought they were on a peaceful mission.
Sergeant Conboy entered the Cafe Royal
and proceeded back to the apartment set
aside for gaming. He watched the men at
the tables, and after satisfying himself
that the "dealer" had taken a percentage
out of the game, he placed the latter and
the men who were gambling under arrest.
When he ordered them into custody the
gamblers started up in affright and tried
to escape. Those playing at the other
tables thinking they, too, would be taken
to prison, made a dash for liberty. Ser
geant Conboy and his assistants made no
effort to detain the fleeing gamblers, be
ing satisfied with those- seated at the un
fortunate table. The latter were hustl.-d
out to the curbstone and placed in the
waiting patrol wagon, along with the
green baize table and other evidence. F.
H. Ryder; Joseph Barry, the game-keeper;
Peter Hayes, Thomas Sonogni and A.
Austin were charged with violating sec
tion 330 of the Penal Code. H. Hornlein,
one of the proprietors of the Cafe Royal,
! having been informed of the visit paid' his
I place by the police, voluntarily visited the
City Prison a half hour aver the raid and
surrendered himself. A similar charge
was placed against him. He put up the
necessary cash bail for the men who
played in his house, and in doing so stated'
that he was protecting his fellow prison
ers.
Hardly had the patrol wagon driven
away from the Cafe Royal when the gam
blers resumed thHr interesting pastime as
if nothing had disturbed them in their ef
forts to win a -ortune.
Officer Esola and posse visited the Met
ropole saloon, which is under the Midway
Plai.sance. Esola duplicated ii.s superior
officer's action by waiting for one of the
players to cash his chips, and he placed
the men under arrest. The table, the
cards and the chips were gathered in,
and another load of players was
carted to the City Prison. The men who
were booked were Frank Winn, \V. Park
er, C. Sipe, Proprietor L. Hanson, Alfred
Berrard, Julius Kline, "Budd" Williams,
and John Mason, the. game-keener.
At the Palace Saloon at 809 Market
street. Officer Tyrrell and posse arrested
its proprietor, ('. W. Robeman. Fred
Myer, Herman Hoffman. Victor Jossljn,
James Wilson, John Wiener, Alfred
Brandenburger and Tom Hanson. A
fimilai charge was placed against the
prisoners.
Setsoont Coogan and posse attempted
to raid the "Our House" at 993 Market,
owned by B. Conway, but before they
could carry their intention into effect
their pr< sence was discovered and no ot
tempt was made to continue 1 the game.
Chief Lees intends swearing to war
rants in the three Police Courts -this
morning, pn that many decisions nu\ he
given by tliu Judges who preside ever

NOTED RAILROAD OFFICIAL.
President Van Home of the Canadian
Pacific Here.
Sir William C. Van Home, president of
the Canadian* Pacific Railway, arrived
from Montreal last evening and registered
at the Pala<v. Mr. Van Home directs the
destinies of one of the greatest systems
â¢ in the world, and is recognized as one of
the most capable men in his line, on 'ac-
SIR WILLIAM CORNELIUS VAN HORNE.
count of his ready grasp of railroad prob
lems and his intimate knowledge of rail
road affairs. He was knighted by Queen
Victoria In 1894.
It Is a fact that Mr. Van Home was an
American citizen before he transferred
his allegiance to the British Government
He was born in Will County. Illinois, in
1813, and his rise from a telegraph op
erator on the Illinois Central road in Chi
cago to his present exalted position is
noteworthy. He filled a score of positions
on various roads, and from 1879 to ISS2 he
was general superintendent of the Chi
cago, Milwaukee and St. Paul. Sixteen
years ago he associated himself with the
Canadian Pacific as general manager and
has been identified with the road since
then. He tills a big place in the commer
cial world of Canada. He is an artist
(both in oils and water colors) of no mean
ability and is a lever of line auetures, of
which he has a large and valuable collec
tion.
